Researchers have discovered that the Notch signaling pathway plays a crucial role in reprogramming germ cells into somatic cells in Caenorhabditis elegans. This is unexpected, as the Notch pathway is known for maintaining undifferentiated germ stem cells/progenitors. Through a combination of genetics, tissue-specific transcript analysis, and functional studies of candidate genes, they found that Notch promotes reprogramming by activating genes silenced by the Polycomb repressive complex 2 (PRC2) and identified the conserved histone demethylase UTX-1 as a Notch target facilitating reprogramming. Notch-activated genes are enriched on the X chromosome, the Caenorhabditis sex chromosome, and nearly all X-linked Not-activated are derepressed upon loss of PR2. This suggests that Notch can induce expression of PR2-repressed genes. Depletion of UTX in the r- background had similar effects on germl as the wild-type, suggesting germl-autonomous function, but some effects were abolished suggesting these function in the soma. Strikingly, Not-activated were enriched on the X-chromosome, the Caenorhabis sex chromosome. Nearly all X-linked Not-activated were derepressed upon loss PR2. This is consistent with genetic interaction and suggests increased Notch can induce PR-repressed genes. Not-activated were also repressed by cell-stabilizing chromatin regulator PR2. They show Notch and PR have antagonistic effect on cell decisions and co-regulate common, ut-1. However, whether these are activated Notch promote differentiation under physiological remains to be determined. Many Not-activated are repressed PR, suggesting expression these determined by cstalk between extracellular signaling, Not, and intrinsic chromatin PR2.
A study investigated the role of the adaptor protein SK2 (SK2) in defense against Klebsiella pneumoniae (K. pneumonia) infection in mice. SK2 is required for protection against K. pneumonia (ATCC 438) pulmonary infections, as SK-/- mice had a 100-fold higher bacterial burden compared to wild-type mice, which was controlled by SK2 expression in innate immune cells. SK2- neutrophils and monocytes were present in lungs and neutils degranulated normally in response to K. pneumonia infection in mice, but K pneumonia-stimulated reactive oxygen (ROS) production in vitro was abolished. K pneumonia-induced neutil ROS required activity of SKs, Sy, B, PLC2, and PK. The loss of SK2 significantly hindered K pneumonia-induced phosphorylation of SK, Sy, and Py, implicating SK2 as proximal to their activation signaling pathways. SK2 signaling in neutils is essential for K pneumonia-activated ROS production and promoting bacterial clearance during infection.

 SK2 was required for full phosphorylation of SK, Sy, and Py in murine neutils, demonstrating a critical role in K pneumonia-induced pathways required for ROS production. Consistent with previous findings, SK plays a key role in host defense against K pneumonia in the hematietic compartment. SK was indispensable for pneumonia-stim neutil ROS, but not degranulation phagocytosis, suggesting the increased burden in SK- mice affected the loss of ROS sensitivity. K pneumonia 16 is resistant to neutil phagocyt regardless of SK expression but elicits SK-independent degran during infection. ROS is a mechanism for protection against pneumonia as mice lacking the gp91ph component of NAD oxidase, - mice were more susceptible to pneumonia.

SK-expressing neutils contributed to pulmonary containment of pneumonia in lungs independent of inflammatory monocytes. Using murine neutils, show K pneumonia-induced production requires SK, SFs, Sy, PLC, B, and PK, and SK2 is required for maximal pneumonia-activated phosphorylation of tyrosine kinases SK, Sy, and Py2. Neutils are defective in triggering cascade required for ROS after pneumonia binding to receptors. SK mediates pneumonia-induced production by influencing tyine phosphorylated functions of SK and Sy. SFs and Sy are phosphorylated in response to pneumonia infection.
Researchers have developed a mathematical model to study the contribution of the hypnoite reservoir to Plasmodium vivax transmission. The model accounts for the activation and death of hypnoites in the liver, which cause relapse infections weeks to months after the initial infection. The model is validated against data from tropical strains of P. viv and embedded in a P. viv transmission model to demonstrate the build-up of the hypite reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hypite activation and death. The hypite reservoir is predicted to be dispersed with individuals having few or no hypites and some intensely infected livers. Individuals with more hypites are predicted to experience relapse and contribute to P. viv transmission. Incorporating hypite drugs such as primaquine into first-line treatment regimens is predicted to cause substantial reductions in P viv transmission as individuals with most hypites are likely to relapse and targeted for treatment.

The hypite reservoir is a third parasite reservoir in addition to the blood and mosquito reservoirs for P falciparum. Relapses can be incorporated into Ross-Mac models through a state representing the hypite reservoir. In the absence of diagnostics for liver-stage parasites, models play a crucial role in estimating the effectiveness of interventions targeting the hypite. Most models of malaria transmission do not account for the liver reservoir, but the hypite has been incorporated into P viv models as a state to denote hypite infection or two broods of hypites.

The within-host model of hypites in hepatocytes demonstrates that many epidemiological patterns of relapse can be explained by constant activation and death of hypites. The model is integrated into Rossac models to account for rel infections of P viv malaria. Following a tropical strain of P viv, the population of hypites in the liver can be described by a model where each hypite is subject to activation and death. Activation and death rates are assumed implying hypite residence in the liver is exponentially distributed. The model is validated against data from tropical strains of P viv.

The model is embedded in a P viv transmission model to demonstrate the build of the hyp reservoir following new infections and its depletion through hyp activation and death. The hyp reservoir is predicted to be dispersed with individuals having few or no hypites and some intensely infectedivers. Individuals with more hypites are predicted to experience relapse and contribute to P viv transmission. Incorporating hyp drugs such as prima into first-line regimens is predicted to cause reductions in P viv as individuals with mostites are likely to relapse and targeted for treatment.

The hyp reservoir can be targeted using drugs such as primaquine that eliminate hypites from the liver. The number of rel hypites in a population exposed to P in the absence of new infections is predicted to decrease exponentially, but the proportion of individuals carrying hypites to decrease at a slower rate as an can rel even have oneite. Considering batches of hypites can explain complex patterns in viv relapses. A key assumption is constant activation of hypites implying rel can occur after primary P infection.
Amazonian forests are disturbed by selective logging at a rate of 2ha per year, releasing 90g of carbon (C) into the atmosphere, which is balanced by forest regrowth. A study of 133 forest plots across Amazonia links climate, soil, and initial biomass with survivors and recruits' carbon fluxes to predict post-logging recovery. Aboveground carbon recovery over 10 years is higher in the Guiana Shield (21 Mg ha-1) than in the south (12 Mg ha-1) where environmental stress is high (low rainfall, high seasonality). Survivors play a key role in regrowth and a comprehensive map of post-disturbance recovery in Amazonia.

The study focuses on selective logging, a long-stud human disturbance in tropical forests, with data from 13 experimentally-disturbed sites in Amazon and Guiana. Results are up-scaled to Amazon using maps of climate, conditions, and soil density, and predict potential changes over 10 years under 40% loss, a common intensity after logging. Climate change draws attention to impacts on carbon cycles, particularly 2.1 Pg yr-1 loss from various disturbances in tropical forests. Selective logging causes post-disturbance dynamics among western, Guiana, and southern Amazon. In western, stress is reduced due to fertile soils and non-seasonal precipitation, but forests are prone to frequent and large-scale wind-induced disturbances.

Survivors contribute to post-disturb recovery. Regions with high survivors' gain (west and northeast) have high net recovery: annual recovery 1-3 Mg ha-1 in first 10 after logging, lower in secondary forests 3-5 ha-1 in 20 after abandonment. The effect of pre-dist ecosystem, disturbance intensity, and environment on post-dist rates is quantified in a Bayesian framework. Post-dist loss from survivors decreases in Guiana, consistent with low mortality and longevity in old-growth. In southern, seasonal stress is a constraint on recovery, stressoler trees are poor competitors. Selectively logged forests remain forested and recover C, previously logged may accumulate C, but uptake is difficult to estimate.

Centralia is a transition for environmental gradients in Amazon: competition between dense and nutrient-poor, and nutrient-rich forests, environmental between wet and drier. Recruits have low variations postlogging changes, 10 after storing similar amounts everywhere. Small trees DB have not been accounted for and may play role in postlogging changes. C from selective is determined by harvest intensity, precipitation, and soil density.
Researchers have identified two distinct populations of neural stem cells in the adult mouse brain's subventricular zone (SV): pre-GEOT cells and GEOT cells. Pre-GEOT cells are quiescent, lack the ability to form neurospheres, and express markers of type 1 cells, while GEOT cells are highly mitotically active, form neuropheres, and express markers of type C cells. GEOT cells account for 3.2% of SV cells and are enriched for neuro-initiating cells (NIC), while pre-GEOT cells are 6% of SV cells and include type 1 cells. Pre-GEOT cells are long-lived in the SV based on fate mapping with stem markers Sl1a-CERT, GF-CERT, Sox2ERT, and GliERT. GEOT cells are short-lived in the SV based on fate mapping with AsclCre2 and DlxCre. Pre-GEOT cells are resistant to temozomide, while GEOT are ablated by temozomide. Conditional deletion of Bmi-1 polycomb protein depletes pre-GEOT and GEOT cells, but pre-GE cells are more dependent on B-1 for Cdk2 (p16ink4) repression. Data distinguish quies NSCs from NIC and make it possible to study their properties in vivo.
Researchers investigated the function of Piezo1 ion channels, a mechanically gated ion channel that senses membrane tension with high sensitivity. Using cell-attached patch-clamp electrophysiology and pressure-clamp, they examined the spatial distribution and functional interactions of Pie1 channels across various densities. Their data, together with stochastic simulations of Pie1 distributions, showed that at endogenous densities (1-2 channels/μm2) and non-physiological densities (10-100/100/μm) predicted substantial overlap. However, Pie1 density had no effect on pressure sensitivity or open probability in the absence of membrane tension. The results suggest Pie channels, at physiologically relevant densities, inherently behave as mechanotrans. This property enables cells to transduce forces homogeneously across the entire membrane.

The study used cell-attached-clamp electrophys to quantify channel number and function, including single current, open probability, and pressure. They sought to characterize the inherent ability of Pie channels to spatially localize and modulate each other's function. Pie1 channels were randomly distributed, with 80% outside a range of 2-3 channels, suggesting no functional interaction via membrane footprints. At high densities (up to 100/μm), Pie1 sensitivity did not change with number. Surprisingly, data suggest in absence of tension, Pie open probability slightly reduced with number, but overall low (<1% at all densities probed. Two analyses showed at densities 1-2/μm and 10-100/μ, Pie pressure and tension not modulated by number. Mechanistic explanation provided by data and model spatial distributions, suggesting NeuroA cells, Pie channels randomly distributed or low aggregating.

To measure and correlate Pie1 density with properties, they performed cell-attached-clamp electroph on NeuroA cells expressing Pie1. Functional interactions/cooperativity between force-gated channels may skew transduction or kinetics, depending on local density, creating patchwork blind and sensitized areas. Recent evidence suggests Pie channels non-uniformly distributed. Distribution fits data well, indicating NeuroA cells, Pie channels homously across surface rather localized. Conclusions applicable to cells expressing Pie similar levels to NeuroA, but specific types may concentrate channels locations detecting forces.

Researchers studied how fruit flies groom their bodies with sequential movements and found that motor programs are organized in a hierarchy. They genetically activated subsets of neurons to drive distinct cleaning programs for individual body parts, demonstrating that programs are organized in a suppression hierarchy. When one body part is cleaned, the sensory drive to its motor is reduced, relieving suppression of the next movement, allowing the sequence to progress down the hierarchy. A model featuring evoked movements in parallel but selected through hierarchical suppression successfully reproduced the grooming sequence. This is the first example of an innate sequence implemented by a model for generating human actions.

 The study found that dust elicits different evoked cleaning programs for body parts that progress in series. By targeting subsets, they gained control over each program to demonstrate they are organized in suppression. An empirically derived model shows how hierarchy can mediate the selection of cleaning programs. Evolutionary ability to select behaviors through suppression may have provided infrastructure to perform actions in sequence. Activation and parallel models describe mechanisms generating sequential behavior, but hierarchical suppression is a feature of parallel models, not chain models. The study organizational underlying control of serial action in an innate behavior, grooming, common to most animals, was investigated.

A significant finding is the demonstration that hierarchical among behaviors leads to emergence of sequence. Identifying neural circuit for each module and how they interact will reveal organization of behavior in nervous system through suppression. The model generates behaviors of complexity. Fruit grooming provides insight into organization of motor sequences. The model progression features proposed to explain sequences that require parallel activation of units competing for output. Each part cleaned, suppression of other modules is lifted, and new competition between remaining occurs. Work supports view that hierarchical is ubiquitous in action mechanism, whether selecting choices or movements in serial tasks.

Grooming displays sequential and cyclic transitions between modules, extending understanding of parallel in serial behavior. Flies make frequent between cleaning and rubbing, oscillations explained by hierarchical rules. Inhibition among motor programs may be due shared circuits, activity of program suppresses another because shared components cannot drive two behaviors. Experiments will address neural mechanisms hierarchical in grooming.
The study investigates the role of Shug-like protein 2 (Sg2) in mammalian oocytes during meiosis I. S2 plays a crucial role in protecting centromeric cohes from separase, promoting bi-orientation of bival chromosomes, and formation of kinetochore-microtub attachments (K-fib). It achieves this by interacting with PP2, a phosphatase that protects cohes from separase, and Mad2, a protein that silences the spindle assembly checkpoint (SAC). S2's ability to protect cohes depends on its interaction with PP2, while its ability to silence SAC is mediated by binding to Mad2. In contrast, its effect on bivalent stretching and Kib formation is independent of PP2 and mediated by recruitment of MCA and inhibition of Aurora kinase activity, respectively. S2's multiple interactions link essential processes for faithful chromosome segregation.

 Depletion of S2 in oocytes has pleiotropic consequences during me I, including inefficient SAC silencing, increased bivalent stretching, and alterations in Kib dynamics. S silences SAC by binding to Mad using a MIM motif common to Mad's partners Cdc20 and Mad1. Delayed biorientation of bival on meind, increased b stretching, and increased Aurora/C activity at kineto, accompanied by increase in lateral and decrease in K attachments. S2 deficiency also delays APC/CC20 activation, suggesting it's required to shut SAC.

The study shows that S2 defective in PP binding fails to protect centrom cohes, unlike S1 in yeast. However, this is not the sole function of S2, as chromosome behavior during I is unaffected. Surprisingly, S2 deficiency causes changes in chromosome and microtule dynamics. Shugins, not S2, have been implicated in recruiting Aurora kinase to kineto for SAC and error correction. S2 has been linked to cohesion, spindle assembly, and error pathways, but physiological significance unclear.

The study investigates association of PP with S2's coiled and its effect on centrom cohesion. Mutations in PP's localization with S2 at kineto and effect on cohesion after me I division show that PP's interaction with S's coiled is necessary for protecting cohes from separase. S2 promotes bi-orientation of homolog chromosomes by increasing K interactions. The study also shows that S2-PPA, not S-Mad, interaction is required for centrom cohesion at me.

The study suggests that multiple processes regulated by S are essential but also regulated by S-independent pathways. S possesses different domains regulating different processes. The study shows that accumulation of S phosphorylated by Aurora stimulates conversion of lateral to end attachments, which may be mechanism S accelerates biorientation.
Neuron-wide RNA transport combines with netrin-mediated local translation to spatially regulate the synaptic proteome. Research has shown that experience-dependent changes in brain connectivity require localized RNA and protein synthesis, with local translation altering synapse structure and function during formation and plasticity. This study investigates whether local stimulation also triggers RNA transport from the nucleus to synapses. Imaging in cultured Aplyia sensory-motor neurons, RNAs were delivered throughout the neuron's arbor, but translation was enriched at sites of contact and stimulation. Investigation of mechanisms revealed a role for calcium-dependent netrin-1/DCC signaling in local translation. Spatially regulating gene expression by translation rather than delivering RNAs to synapses maximizes the neuron's readiness to respond to local cues.

 The study found that RNAs, ribosomal proteins, and translation factors were delivered throughout the neuron, but translation was significantly at synaptic and stimulated sites. This indicates regulation of gene expression in Aplyia sensory neurons is primarily at the level of translation, not RNA targeting from the nucleus. Overexpression of recombinant netrin-1 in MNs increased synaptic strength and sensorin immunoreactivity. Netrin-1 is a retrograde signal driving translation in the neuron, promoting axonal growth, synaptogenesis, and transmembrane regulation in dendrites and axons. Transcripts, ribosomes, and translational machinery were delivered throughout the neuron, but translation was selectively activated in response to stimuli.

 The study used the Aplyia-MN culture system to monitor mRNA localization and translation during synapse formation and plasticity, finding the proteome regulated at the level of translation rather than stimulus-induced targeting. Netrin-1 was able to stimulate translation at non-syn sites, suggesting a large number of transcripts localize throughout the neuron. The subcell localization of mRNA is secondary to the localization of the netrin signal, not in the synaptic per se to be translated. The study shows a pathway in axon guidance regulates synapse by spatially restricting gene expression, further uncoupling transcriptional and translational regulation in neurons.

A normative theory of set effects in human visual working memory proposes that encoding precision decreases with the number of items due to a trade-off between behavioral cost and neural cost. A model based on this theory predicts size effects in delayed-estimation tasks, which are mediated by probability, aligning with empirical findings. The model accounts for effects of size and probability on precision in nine experiments and shows that the amount of resource invested varies non-monotonically with size. It is optimal to encode a subset or none of items in some cases, raising the possibility that limitations arise from cost minimization rather than constraints.

 The theory provides a principled explanation for selectivity of attention, balancing performance against spiking costs, and accounts for size effects in attention tasks. Tested on data from nine experiments, it well accounts for size and probability effects with few parameters. The model predicts a nonotonic relation between population spiking and size, which can be tested with neuroimaging experiments. The theory also makes predictions about incentive manipulations on precision, affecting the expected cost function.

 The model assumes no lower limit on resource for encoding, but there is evidence for automatic encoding of task-irrelevant stimuli in natural environments. However, it is optimal to assign zero resource to an item when probability is lower than a threshold. The theory optimizes on a trial-by-trial basis, unlike neural coding which optimizes over timescales. It fits into a tradition of normative theories in psychology and neuroscience.

 The model is tested on three experiments with varied probability and size, accounting for size and probability effects. It does not depend on size, but accounts for size in experiments where size is coupled with probability. The model predicts size effects in delayedest tasks are mediated by individual probability, consistent with observations. It is speculated that chunking is optimal in the model, reducing expected cost and increasing behavioral cost. Developmental changes in working capacity raise the question of optimal trade-off between behavioral and neural cost.

 A form of normalization is a candidate, lowering gain with size without explicit knowledge of size. It is optimal to encode a subset in change detection tasks, unlike delayedest. Simulations are intractable at larger sizes. The model accounts for data in variety of delayedest experiments.
Researchers investigated how the Irish potato pathogen Phytophthora infest interferes with plant autophagy, a process used by plants to defend against diseases. They found that the pathogen's effector Pex54 binds to the autoph protein AT8, stimulating autag formation and depleting the autagy receptor J2 out of AT8 complexes to counter host defenses. This is the first time a pathogen has been shown to antagonize a host autagy receptor for its benefit. P54 competes with host receptor J2 and depletes it out AT8 autagosomes to promote susceptibility. The study shows pathogens can activate autagy for their own benefit, not just disrupt it. Autagy plays a role in stress, senescence, development, and defense against pathogens, and is implicated in the accumulation of defense hormones and hypersensitive response, a form of cell death that prevents microbial spread. Overexpression of J2, but not J2AIM, restricted disease caused by P. infans, and virus-induced silencing of J2 increased disease. The path delivers RX-type effector proteins inside cells to parasitize, and P. infans is a threat to food security, causing losses that alleviated could feed millions.
Researchers have developed a novel in vitro model to study human cardiac fibroblast (CF) responses to combined mechanical strain and oxygen changes, mimicking pathological conditions such as ischemia. They used a multi-stimulus device to apply cyclic strain and controlled oxygen tension to primary human CFs, revealing differential responses to individual and combined stimuli. Cyclic strain caused proliferative effects, including PH3+ mitotic cells, YAP translocation, PD secretion, and collagen presence. However, only the combination of hypoxia and simulated loss of contractility (2% strain) induced increased inflammatory and pro-frotic cytokines and matrix metalloproteinases. The model recreates a pathological environment for understanding CF responses during cardiac injury, replicating for the first time the combination of oxygen and mechanical cues, highlighting emerging cellular mechanisms. CFs have been studied under physicochemical stimulation such as mechanical stress, oxygen deprivation, and biochemical with pro-frotic cytokines, but not simultaneously. This model provides insights into synergistic contributions of environmental signals in activation of early CF responses relevant to cardiacrot disease. After ischemic myocardial insult, oxygen levels drop to 5-6% and excessive CF proliferation and phenotypic switch occur. To understand environmental modulation of CF proliferation, mitotic cells (PH3+/DA) were examined under mechanical and oxygen changes. The combination of H and reduced contractility elicited inflammatory and fibrot responses, while individual stimuli regulated proliferative and collagen responses. This has impact on future studies of myocardial remodeling and the model provides a tool for understanding pathological mechanisms and reparative strategies.
In Drosophila melanogaster, two isoforms of the DOM nucleosome remodeling ATPase, DOM-A and DOM-B, specify distinct multi-subunit complexes that are necessary for transcriptional regulation through different mechanisms. The DOM-B complex incorporates H2.V, the fly ortholog of H2.Z, genome-wide in an ATP-dependent manner, similar to the SWR complex in yeast. DOM-A, instead, functions as an ATP-independent histone acetylase complex similar to yeast Nu4, targeting lysine 12 of histone H4. This work illustrates how different evolutionary strategies can lead to similar functional separation, as seen in yeast and humans where nucleosome and histone acetylase complexes originate from gene duplication and paralogy. Dophila generates diversity by alternative splicing of a single gene.

 The DOM-A features domains are separated by a long ATPase domain, raising the possibility that DOM might serve as the central subunit of a Nu4-type complex in Dophila. The existence of such a complex with functional and structural similarity to the well-stud yeast complex has not been reported. Surprisingly, data suggest the ATP activity of DOM is dispensable for H4 acylation by DOM, a principle conserved across metazoa. Work illustrates alternative splicing generates functional diversity amongst chromatin regulators. H2.Z is found at active and enhancers, regulating initiation and elongation. In Saccharces cerevisiae, H.Z is introduced chromatin by SWR complex, a multiunit ATP-dependent remodeler with IN80-type ATPase SWR at core. Doph DOM-B is likely employ similar mechanism. Knock of DOM affects transcription, effects partially overlap with HAV depletion.

 DOM-A is not involved in HAV incorporation, despite presence of ATP domain and shared subunits with DOM. Instead, DOM might be the missing acetylase Nu4 of D melanaster, specifically targeting lysine 12 histone H4. Biochemical characterization revealed presence of multiunit composed 15 proteins with ATP. Many interactors are orthous to yeast SW subunits, additional interactors showed similarity to distinct complex Nu Acetylase of H4 Nu4. Nu4 is responsible histone acylation. Researchers investigated the role of β-catenin (Ctn1) in muscle in neuromuscular junction (NMJ) formation, specifically focusing on the retrograde signal from muscle to neuron. They used an in vivo genetic approach to dissect the functions of Ctn1 and found that its transcriptional activity is necessary for presynaptic differentiation. The cell-adhesion function of C1 is dispensable, suggesting that C1 regulates presaptic differentiation by controlling the expression of muscle-derived proteins. They identified Slit2 as a target of C1-regulated transcription in muscles and showed that overexpressing Sl2 in muscles rescues presaptic deficits caused by C1 mutation. Sl2 is a member of the Sl family of secreted glycoproteins that repels axons and regulates axon fasculation. Sl2 induces synaptophys puncta in axons of spinal cord explants and rescues NM deficits in C1-mut mice. The study suggests that Sl2 serves as a factor utilized by C1 to direct presaptic differentiation.
Researchers have developed a method to quantify the dynamics of force transmission at cell-cell junctions in epithelial clusters using computational and experimental approaches. They applied this method to spontaneously forming adherent epithelial cell clusters and found that basal fluctuations in force were coupled to E-cadher localization at the level of individual cell junctions. At the multi-cell scale, cell force transmission depended on cell position within the cluster and was adaptive to reconfigurations due to cell divisions or positional rearrangements. Importantly, transmission through a cell required modulation of cell-matrix adhesion and actomy contractility in the cell and its neighbors. The data provide insights into mechanisms that control mechanical homeostasis in epithelial tissues and highlight the method as a resource for study mechanotrans in cell adhesions.

The researchers used a cell-cell force measurement method to quantify spatially and temporally the transmission of forces through cell-cell junctions of adherent epithelial clusters. Time-resolved measurements showed that cell-cell forces at junctions fluctuate over time during interphase and cell divisions. They investigated how basal variations were coupled to the localization of molecular components of cell junctions, focusing on Eadher, the backbone of adherens junction for epithelial cells. Along the junction, local stresses correlated with Ead localization. Expanding the approach to force fluctuation at the scale of cell, force through cells can be intercepted by anchorage to the extracellular and actomy contraction.

The method provides insights into tissue mechanics by revealing force transmission across cell is short-ranged, typically on the scale of cell diameter due to anchoring and scrambling forces by cell-matrix adhes and actomy contractility. Researchers have identified a homin-specific gene, TBCD1, that plays a crucial role in the generation of basal progenitor cells and cortical folding in mice. Expression of TBCD in ventricular progenitors via in utero electroporation caused delamination of ventricular glial cells and promoted the generation of self-renew basal progenitors with morphology of radial glia, which are abundant in primates. Downregulation of T1 in human brain slices decreased generation of basal progenitors. Localized basal proliferation resulting from in utero electoration or transgenic expression of T1 was found to underlie cortical regions exhibiting folding. The study provides a proof-of-concept that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during homin evolution. Cortical expansion and folding are linked to evolution of higher intelligence, but molecular and cellular mechanisms remain poorly understood. TBCD undergoes segmental duplications during homin evolution, but its role in brain has not been explored. The study found a remarkable effect of T in promoting generation of basal cells in mice with proliferative ability observed in primates. Importantly, basal generation is accompanied by appearance of cortical folding. A human gene ARHG11 has been shown to promote basal production and cortical in electropated brain. T, which appeared earlier than ARHG11 during evolution, acts as a candidate that controls genes involved in expansion and folding. The study provides evidence that brain development can be regulated by duplicated genes during homin evolution.
The study investigates the role of ubiquitination in the transcriptional activity of the plant immune coactivator NPR1, a key regulator of systemic acquired resistance in plants. Ubiquitination of NPR is thought to be a sacrificial process, leading to its proteasomal degradation, but the study shows it is a progressive event with initial modification by Cullin-RING E3 ligase promoting chromatin association and expression of target genes. Polyubiquination by E4 ligase U4 targets NPR for proteasomal degradation, while deubiquases UBP/7 enhance NPR longevity. The study demonstrates that initial ubiquination by C3 is required for NPR to attain full transcriptional activity, while stepwise formation of ubiqu chains by U4 inactivates NPR and promotes degradation by the proteasome. U4 is shown to act in concert with C3 to regulate NPR, suggesting a single E enzyme may assist ubiqu-mediated pathways by different E ligases. Trimming or removal of ubiqu chains is performed by deubitinases, providing another layer of regulation of NPR activity. In the absence of U4, NPR remains in a highly transcriptional state, suggesting U4 exerts effects through NPR. The study shows that opposing actions of E and E ligase and Ds fine-tune transcription outputs of unstable coactivator NPR without strict requirement for sacr turnover. Dynamic conjugated ubiqu chain length may be a mechanism controlling activity of unstable eukotic coactivators in general.

Researchers investigated the development of neural networks in the brain, focusing on the transition from immature to mature patterns in rodents and humans. They used high-resolution electrophysiology to record activity in somatosensory cortex of mouse pups from postnatal day 5 to 14, a period spanning the transition from immature to mature patterns during sleep. Results showed that cortical dynamics shifted to temporally precise neural spiking after a discrete, relatively quiescent period at the beginning of the second postnatal week. Synchronized firing within 10-50 ms intervals was absent prior to this period and robustly emerged afterward. This is consistent with increased pair-wise correlations of spike activity in visual cortex development. A developmental inflection may reflect rapid engagement of mechanisms enabling mature processes within networks. Analysis of electroencephalography (EEG) recordings from human subjects 36-69 weeks post- gestation revealed a similar trajectory, characterized by transient decrease in activity before onset of spatiemp oscillatory coupling. A shift from local, loosely, sensory-driven patterns to organized, spatially distributed, and temporally precise activity preceded a quiescent period. Oscillations transformed at the beginning of the second postnatal, potentially a transition in network from highly localized processing to broader recruitment of activity. Oscill in adult organize neural across spatial and temporal scales. LFP and spiking measures followed a nonlinear trajectory marked by a transition at the beginning the postnatal in pups. A similar pattern of maturation was observed in humans, suggesting an evolutionarily conserved mechanism. A transient quies period may be requisite for emergence of coordinated networks.
Researchers have developed an agent-based model that extends earlier strain theory to address the effect of immune selection on multi-copy antigen-encoding genes in Plasium falciparum. The model considers the extensive diversity of multi-copy genes that undergo antigenic variation via sequential, mutually exclusive expression and tracks the dynamics of all unique reperto in a population of hosts. It shows that even under high levels of sexual recombination, strain-mediated competition through cross-immunity structures the population into a subset of coexisting reperto of genes whose degree of antigen overlap depends on transmission intensity. Empirical comparison of patterns of genetic at antigenic and neutral sites supports the role of immune in structuring parasite diversity.

 The model considers the effects of immune in populations of pathogens whose antigen-loci are multi-copy and recombining. It explores the shaping of the enormous diversity of reperto of genes by tracking all possible rep, given a diverse pool of variants. The model shows that structured populations resulting from selection exerted by competition among rep for hosts, mediated by cross-immunity can emerge. The model is based on the var family of P. falcum, which encodes P. falc erythrocyte membrane 1, the major antigen of the parasite's blood stages that binds to endothelial receptors to sequest within host tissues.

 The model demonstrates the emergence and maintenance of population structure under three levels of recombination, 0.001, 0.01, and 0.1, while keeping other parameters equal. It shows that decreasing transmission provokes nonlinear changes in dominant configurations. Importantly, results demonstrate there is not a simple relationship between diversity and repertoire. The model provides a plausible explanation for why it is difficult to eliminate malaria: non-overlapping diversity creates inherent difficulties in reducing potential to reinfect same hosts. It suggests var might provide a molecular means evaluating control.

 Each genome has up 60 genes, and sequential expression allows to evade host response and establish chronic. This clonic variation extends duration to facilitate transmission to mosquito. Limits random mating and recombination between genomes. Even under neutral, parasites differ in relatedness. The model constrains repertoire to levels orders lower than possible given diversity. Reduction occurs coexisting into variants largely discordant reperto. When transmitted from host to mosquito, there is a probability that reoccurs, resulting in new repertoire transmitted to second host.
Researchers investigated the mechanism by which the bacterial effector protein OI from Shigella flexner inactivates the host cell's inflammatory response by deamating a glutamine residue in the ubiquitin-conjugating enzyme UBC, converting it to glutamate. This disrupts the interaction between UBC and the R-finger ubiquitin ligase TR6, silencing the NF-κ inflammatory response. The study found that deidation inhibits the interaction between U13 and TR6 by three mechanisms: a new intramolecular saltbridge in U13 competes with a critical intermolecular bridge at the native interface, b saltbridge also perturbs transient complexes to inhibit association, and repulsion between negatively charged E and negatively surface TR6 reduces transient. Deid does not alter the fold of U13 but creates an intram saltbridge that competes with interm salt at the native interface. The study used NMR spectroscopy, molecular dynamics simulations, and in-vit ubiquination assays to show deid weakens noncoval interaction between U13 and TR6 domain of TR6 without perturbing U13 or intrinsic activity. The findings provide insights into the molecular mechanism behind inactivation of the ubiquitin pathway by deation. The researchers also tested the effect of deation on another R domain, R38, indicating the mechanism could be ubiquitous in U13/R complexes.
Researchers investigated the effects of optogenetic activation and inactivation of somatostatin-positive (S+) and parvalbum-positive (Pb) interneurons in the auditory cortex of awake mice using single-unit recordings. They found that inactivation of S+ interneurons increased response gain, while inactivation of P+ interneurons weakened tuning and decreased information transfer, implying these neurons support specific functions. However, activating S+ and P+ interneurons revealed no differences. A simple model was used to understand this asymmetry, showing that small changes in parameters such as spontaneous activity or light strength determined whether activation and inactivation would produce consistent or paradoxical conclusions about interne functions. Optogenetic activation and inactivation did not produce symmetric results, contradicting the notion that increasing and decreasing interne activity strengthens and weakens, respectively, their functions. The study suggests that activation and inactivation may differentially affect how interne-specific inhibition is propagated through the network. Activation of S+ and+ interne produced no differences in sensory processing, even where activation caused strong suppression, implying S+ and+ function equivalently in these aspects. A fraction of units showed changes in baseline activity despite no tone responses. The study proposes that activation may mask interne function by targeting inactive neurons and obscure distinctions, while inactivation targets active and preserves.
Researchers used transcranial alternating current stimulation (tACS) to externally modulate oscillatory activity in the right frontopari network, which supports working memory (WM). They found that externally induced synchronization improved performance when cognitive demands were high. Functional MRI data revealed that ACS effects depended on the relative phase of stimulation and internal processing. Specifically, synchronous ACS during WM increased parietal activity, which correlated with behavioral performance. Functional results indicate that the phase of frontopetal stimulation influences information flow within WM. The study demonstrates a link between performance in a WM task and large-scale brain synchronization.

 The researchers tested four hypotheses: 1) WM performance is enhanced by entraining activity in the rightop network; 2 regional activity is modulated in a phase manner; 3 changes in BOLD by phase-dependent stimulation correlate with performance; 4 connectivity across brain regions is modulated in a phase. Frontal interactions increased with synchronous stimulation, whereas posterior increased with desynchronous, suggesting information flow can be differentially affected by phase. They provide direct evidence for the role of phase in WM processes and neural correlates.

ACS improved performance and increased frontopetal activity when demands were high, replicating previous findings that synchronous stimulation improves WM. However, effects were dependent on task demands, suggesting interactions with the extent network engaged. Synchronous activity increased theta between frontal and pariet regions during WM. Neuroimaging results show neural correlates of this effect, with increased activity in inferior parietal and middle gyrus, involved in WM. Modulations were restricted to areas involved in performance, no effects in cortical underneath the electrode.

ACS modulated functional connectivity between IPL and frontal parts of the frontop network, increasing efficiency. Results demonstrate external manipulations of oscillations impact activity and behavior, dependent on phase and cognitive state. Synchronous stimulation improved WM, increased frontal and parietal activity, and modulated parietal connectivity. Rhythmic synchrony increases network efficiency, relevant for demanding processes like WM. Front and pariet regions support WM.
Researchers have identified the transcription factor Pitx as a key regulator of embryonic axis formation and twinning in chick embryos. In contrast to invertebrates and amphibians, where embryonic polarity is specified by maternal determinants, amni embryos retain a regulative model until gastrulation. A molecular screen and bioinformatic analysis identified Pitx as a direct regulator of cV1, a growth factor expressed posteriorly before gastrulation. Pitx is essential for axis formation and acts by binding to enhancers within the c1 locus. Pit, c1, and Nodal are also involved in left-right asymmetry, suggesting an ancient polarity mechanism has been co-opted for different functions during evolution. In chick embryos, c1 is first detected at stage XI, while Pitx is expressed in the marginal zone by the time of laying, remaining until early stages. Loss-of-function experiments show Pitx is required for c1 activation and axis formation in normal development and twinning, and binds to non-coding regions around the c1 locus. Pitx is strongly co-regulated with c1 and top transcription factor in the marginal zone, upregulated 10-fold compared to anterior explants. Two screens dissected marginal and anterior explants from embryos and analyzed transcriptomes using Affymrix microarrays, identifying Pitx as a candidate upstream regulator of c1. Pit1 is barely detectable in marginal at stages-XII, suggesting redundancy or compensatory upregulation in response to Pit2 knockdown. The left-right pathway may have co-opted an ancient mechanism for gastr axis formation, supported by Nodal/Pit2 involvement in specifying left-right asymmetry and mesoderm in sea urchin.

In flowering plants, seed development is initiated by the fusion of the maternal egg and central cells with paternal cells, leading to the formation of the embryo and endosperm, respectively. The fertilization products are surrounded by the maternally derived coat, whose development prior to fertilization is blocked by epigenetic regulators belonging to the Polycomb (Pc) protein family. This study shows that fertilization of the central cell results in production of auxin, likely exported to the maternal tissues, driving coat development by removing Pc function. Mutants for the MADS transcription factor AGL have impaired auxin transport from endos to integuments, resulting in seed abortion. It is proposed that AGL regulates auxin transport from endos to integ, leading to removal of Pc block on seed development.

 Auxin accumulates in integuments after fertilization, and impaired biosynthesis but not signaling in endperm causes defects in seed development. Ovules of transgenic lines producing auxin ectopically in central initiate coat development without fertilization. Failure of coat in AGL mutant correlates with failure of endperm development. Seed development requires production of aux in fertilized central/endperm and likely transport to ovules in AGL-dependent manner. Fertilization of central results in production of aux exported to integ, lifting P2 block on seed development allowing elongation and differentiation. However, this is dependent on activity of transcription factor AGL, as A62 seeds fail to export aux from endperm leading to accumulation and failure to develop coat.

A62 is important for expression and activity of endperm-specific aux-exporters. Ectopic production of aux in central is sufficient to drive seed development and A62 regulates expression of PGP10 in endperm, which functions as aux to sporophytic tissues. Aux is sufficient to remove P2 block on seed development. Collectively, data support hypothesis that aux generated in fertil central/endperm is trigger seed formation. Impaired signaling in endperm did not seed, supporting that aux exported to integ initiates downstream events.

Researchers used optogenetic mapping in mice to investigate the neural circuits of the granular retrosplenial cortex (RSG), a region critical for both spatial and non-spatial behaviors. They found a dissociation between parallel circuits in superficial SG, with anterior thalam and dorsalic sources of spatial information selectively recruiting low-rhease (LR) pyramidal cells, while neighboring regular-spiking (RS) cells were preferentially controlled by clastral and cingulate inputs, sources of non-spatial information. Precise subaminar and dendritic arbor within SG layer 1 permitted parallel processing. Observed thalamocort synaptic dynamics enabled LR neurons to compute speed of head rotation, despite receiving direction inputs not explicitly encoding speed. Parallel streams identified a principal subtype positioned to support spatial computations in SG.

The study highlighted a retrosial circuit enabled by precise laminar organization of principal subtypes and afferents, with LR receiving directional and spatial inputs from anterioralam and dorsalic. Synaptic dynamics of thamic inputs to cells gave rise to rate coding of angular speed in LR cells. Determining integration of parallel streams in downstream neurons within ipsilateral and contralateral gran and granular dysgranular SG is crucial for understanding retrosial computations. Thalamort inputs preferentially controlled LR, while clastr and culate controlled RS. Synaptic dynamics of thamic allowed robust encoding of angular by LR models, despite inputs only containing direction not speed.

LR pyramidal cells in SG contained two types: low-rase (LR) and regular-sp (RS), with distinct morphology and intrinsic properties. LR integrated direction from thalam and spatial from hippocampic via dorsalic. Integration of directional and distance was key function of retrosial as whole. LR were defining feature of SG. Electrophys properties of LR played integral role in R's capacity to process thamic direction. Researchers proposed hypotheses for LR targets and function: LR received unilateral inputs from directional in anterioralam, encoding speed and direction, transmitted to RS in contralateral but not ipsilateral SG. Results suggested LR positioned to contribute spatial computations in superficial SG. Synaptic depression introduced angular signal into LR, producing responsive to faster head, supporting orientation functions attributed SG.
Researchers have identified a potential link between traumatic brain injury (TBI) and the development of tauopathies such as chronic traumatic encephalopathy (CTE). They used zebraf larvae to study the role of seizures in the spread of tau pathology after TBI. The larvae were engineered to express a fluorescent biosensor that reports the accumulation of human Tau when seeded with intraventricular brain injections. After TBI, the larvae exhibited cell death, post-traumatic seizures, and Tau inclusions. The application of dynamin inhibitors or anticonvulsant drugs rescued T-induced tauopathy and cell death, suggesting a role for seizure activity in the prion seeding and spreading of tauopathy following T. The study found a burst of neural activity during T, and zraf larvae exposed to T had a higher propensity for spontaneous seizures. Validation of the method induced TBI in zraf larvae showed that T induces Tau aggregates in the biosensor model. Combining approaches, post-tra seizures correlated with spreading pathology following T. Manipulating seizure activity mitigated Tau aggregation, revealing a role for endocyt in the pr-like spread of Tau in vivo T. The results implicate seizures and dyn-dependent endocyt in the spread of Tau, offering therapeutic targets.
Researchers investigated the relationship between the spatial configuration and functional connectivity of brain regions using resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (rfMRI) data. They found that the shape and location of brain regions interact with measures of connectivity and that spatial arrangement of functional regions is strongly predictive of non-imaging measures of behavior and lifestyle. The study suggests that cross-subject variations in spatial configuration of brain are often interpreted as changes in functional connectivity. The researchers used Canonical Correlation Analysis (CCA) to extract modes of-subject covari that represent correlations between behavioral and fMRI measures, uncovering multivariate relationships between brain and behavior. Results show that spatial features such as P subject maps and task maps are strongly associated with behavior. 

The study used data from the Human Connectome Project (HCP) and found systematic differences in the organization of functional regions. The presence of behaviorally spatial variability is surprising because data were aligned using Multimodal Matching (MSM) approach driven by structural and cortical features. Researchers identified a key aspect of rf data that reflects variability in behavior and lifestyle across individuals. Results indicate spatial in topography of regions is associated with behavior. The findings have implications for future rf research and interpretation of findings. The study highlights the need for methods that accurately capture functional in subjects.

Researchers used a large-scale optogenetic interface to stimulate excitatory neurons and record electrocortic activity across primary somatosensory (S1) and motor (M) cortex in macaques. They tracked two measures of network connectivity, the response to stimulation and baseline between pairs of electrodes, which were strongly correlated before stimulation. Within minutes, stimulation in S or M significantly strengthened functional connectivity between areas. At a finer scale, stimulation led to heterogeneous changes across the network, reflecting correlations introduced by stimulation-evoked activity, consistent with Hebbian plasticity models. This work extends Hebbian plasticity to large circuits, with implications for stimulation-based neurorehabilitation.

The researchers investigated how stimulation impacts connectivity and within areas, testing whether stimulation-evoked drives large-scale plasticity in a Hebb manner. They compared two measures of functional connectivity, providing complementary views of mechanisms. Hebb rules have been used to explain synaptic plasticity, so they explored large stimulation-induced changes consistent with Hebb plastic. The strongest test of Hebb model is comparing correlations induced by stimulation with changes in network observed after conditioning. They recorded artifact-free signals during stimulation, allowing this test.

Despite differences, both support a key role for Hebb in sensorimotor cortical plasticity. Understanding the relationship requires study of intermediate timeframes and cortical mapping. The large interface enabled robust estimation of functional and stimulation-evoked correlations. Optogen stimulation at a single location strengthens inter-area connectivity between brain areas, similar to single-site electrical stimulation. Stimulation-induced long-term potentiation (LTP) and STDP have been within local circuits, posited as mechanism stimulation-induced within M. This study explains large changes across networks.

The innate immune sensor IF16 recognizes foreign DNA in the nucleus by scanning along the duplex. Researchers used biochemical and single-molecule techniques to show that IF16 one-dimensionally tracks long stretches of exposed dsDNA to assemble into supramolecular signaling platforms. Nucleosomes represent barriers that prevent IF16 from targeting host DNA by interfering with one-dimensional movements. This unique scanning-assisted mechanism allows IF16 to distinguish from foe and assemble oligomers efficiently on foreign DNA.

 IF16 is a key immune sensor that detects dsDNA and uses as a scaffold to assemble supram signaling in the nucleus and cytoplasm. It plays a central role in defense against pathogens such as herpes simplex virus-1. Researchers report a unifying mechanism that explains how IF resolves central issues in its foreign-dsensing pathways. IF16 assembles on longer dsDNA, composed of three domains flanked by unstructured linkers, namely pyrin and ds-binding domains. The 1-diffusion mediated plays a role in regulating overall IF16 responses.

Chromatin acts as a feature that allows IF to distinguish from DNA in the nucleus. IF oligizes exposed foreign-ds before becomes hetero-chatin. Results support that chromatin is a deterrent preventing assembly IF signaling on selfs.

The counters infection in the nucleus by assembling supram platforms trigger responses on invading but not own material.

Researchers have developed a novel proteomics technique called spatially targeted microproteomics (ST) that allows for the interrogation of micron-scale regions of interest (ROIs) in mammalian tissue without genetic manipulation. Methanol or formalin-fixed specimens are stained with fluorescent dyes or antibodies to visualize ROIs, then soaked in solutions containing the photo-tag 4-benzylbenz-glyc-histidine. Confocal imaging with two-photon excitation is used to covalently photo-tag proteins within each ROI to a resolution of 0.67 µm in the xy-plane and 1.48m axially. After solubization, photo-tagged proteins are isolated by mass spectrom. As a test, amyloid plaques in an Alzheimer's disease (AD) mouse and post-mortem case were examined, confirming known constituents and discovering new. ST can be applied to various samples including cell lines, primary cultures, ex vivo, specimens, biopsy, and post-mort tissue.

ST combines two-photon laser microscopy with photochemical labeling and mass spectrom. Two-ph excitation has been used for microscopic 3D-resolved photochemistry. The predominant photo-tagged protein in ST sample A is, which is not visible in non-plaque due to low abundance. The photo-tag procedure uses two-ph microscopy rather than confocal. ST represents the first application of this technique to affinity labeling, offering single-mic-scale three-dimensional resolution an order of magnitude better than current state-of-the-art LCM. In addition to neurodegenerative disease etiology, this technique has potential to advance cell biology by yielding composition small features not amenable biochemical.

The technique hinges on selective photo-labeling and purification, requiring assessment of non-specific 6BP in light and immunofluorescence excitation, as well as non-binding affinity beads. Immunofluores staining the section with anti-H6 after photoactivation superposes on digital, highlighting high accuracy of two-ph laser. ST combines microscopy with selective-label to resolve, capture, and-label irregular micron structures via semi-automated procedure. The specific labeling permits sensitive detection of proteins in plaques, even abundant in remainder brain. Determination of ST RO as small as 0.38m can be analyzed. Protein via massrom involved onad digestion photo with endoprotein L-C followed tryp and LCMS analysis.

Disruption of the Fz gene leads to defects in axonal growth in the VIIth and XIIth cranial nerves, phrenic nerve, and dorsal nerve in fore and hindlimbs. Fz−/− axons stall at a precise location in the plexus and do not reroute to other trajectories. Affected motor neurons undergo cell death 2 days prior to normal developmental cell death, coinciding innervation of muscle, providing evidence that developing neurons with long axons are programmed to die unless axons arrive at targets on schedule. Experiments implicate planar cell (PC) signaling in axon growth and highlight question how PC proteins function in dynamic axonal context.

In the limb, absence Fz leads to specific stalling among LM axons distinct from phenotypes produced by other signaling systems. Affected cran and spinal neurons die shortly after axons, providing evidence that axons relay survival signals to bodies. The study asks whether primary cause of ax deficiency in VII, XII, phic, and limb in Fz− mice: (1) motor differentiation, (2) death, or (3) growth/guidance. Since F− ax do not follow erroneous trajectories, aberrant pathfinding is unlikely. Researchers have investigated the evolution of C4 photosynthesis, a complex trait that has emerged independently in at least 60 plant lineages. They conducted a meta-analysis of 18 lineages containing species with C3, C, or intermediate C-3-C forms of photosynthesis to parameterize a 16-dimensional phenotypic landscape. A novel Bayesian approach based on a hidden Markov model was developed and experimentally verified to predict the evolution of the C4 phenotype. The study found that the polyphyletic appearance of C4 photosynthesis is associated with diverse and flexible evolutionary paths that group into four major trajectories. The evolutionary histories underlying the appearance of C4 photosynthesis were determined by ancestral and initial phenotypic alterations unrelated to photosynthesis. The order of C trait acquisition is flexible and driven by non-photosynthetic drivers. This flexibility has facilitated the convergent evolution of the complex trait.

The study used data from 43 studies encompassing 18 C, 18, and 37 C-C intermediate species from 22 genera. The phenotyp landscape underlying the evolution of C photosynthesis was modelled as a transition network with edges describing the probability of transitions between phenotyp states. Observed intermediates were used to constrain the structure of the phenotyp landscapes. The number of molecular alterations required for the evolution of C photos is unknown, and the study aimed to test if multiple traits were predicted to evolve linked and mediated by a single mechanism. A contingency analysis was performed considering trajectories across phenotype beginning a given acquisition. The posterior distributions for the time of each phenotyp trait were combined to produce a computationally generated blueprint for the order of events generating C photos. The results were consistent with previous work on subsets of C lineages proposing the specificity of GDC occurs prior to evolution of C metabolism.

The evolutionary events generating C photos on multiple occasions have potential to inform approaches to engineer C into C species. The C pathway is estimated to have evolved between 32 and 25 million years ago in response to ecological drivers including decreasing CO concentration. The study combined concept of evolutionary paths as processes on complex landscapes with analysis of C-C species to develop a predictive of the full C phenotype. The flexibility in underlying mechanisms may be a factor in the convergent evolution of complex traits.
Researchers studied the bacterial protein SpoIVA, a cytoskeletal protein that hydrolyzes ATP to form polymers during Bacillus subtilis sporulation. SpoIVA evolved from a TRAC class P GTPase, but the evolutionary pressure driving the change in nucleotide specificity is unclear. They reengineered the nucleotide pocket of Spo to mimic its ancestral GTP activity, SpoAGTP, which functioned as a GTPase but failed to polymerize because it did not form an NDP intermediate required for polymerization. Incubation of SpoAG with ATP did not promote polymerization, revealing that the nucle base, in addition to energy from hydrolysis, can be critical for biological functions. Data suggest that increased ATP levels relative to GTP at the end of sporulation drove the change in nucle preference in Spo.

SpoAGTP, functional in vivo, could use either ATP or GTP to induce conformation change after hydrolysis, but polymerized only in presence of ATP, indicating a specific requirement for the base, not energy from hydrolysis. Levels of intracellular ATP, not G, facilitated conformation changes in Spo and SpoAG. Both Spo and SpoAG failed hydroly-mediated changes in presence 0.06 GTP, but did so in presence 1.5 ATP. Examining Spo polymerization at different ATP concentrations, neither Spo nor SpoAG polymerized in presence 0, but did in presence 1.5.

The study aimed to understand why Spo, ancestrally derived from Era-like Gase, a pan-bacterial ribosomal protein, had evolved to use ATP instead of GTP. Monitoring hydrolysis-driven conformation and polymerization in vitro, they found that Spo used either ATP or G to induce conformation change, but only polymerized in presence of ATP. Spo localized similar to WT, consistent with ability to support spor at WT level. Disrupting the pocket to preferentially hydroly G over ATP resulted in protein that retained function in vivo. However, hydroly of G drove conformation in Spo similar to ATP, but did not permit polymer or retain GDP.

Intracellular levels of ATP, not G, at end spor drove the evolution of Spo to use ATP for polymerization, a critical in morphogenesis of spore surface. Incubation SpoAG with ATP, but not G, shifted population into void, indicating reengine SpoAG retained dependence on ATP for polymerization. Results suggest a model where Spo, at low concentration, hydrolyzes ATP to conformation and assembles into multimers that retain ADP, then releases AD and forms nucle-free polymer.
A study investigated the long-term consequences of the absence of leptin signaling in early life by restoring leptin signaling at the fourth or tenth week of age in mice null for the leptin receptor (Lep) gene. The results showed that defects previously considered irreversible due to neonatal deficiency of leptin signaling, including poor development of arcuate neural projections, were recovered by Lep reactivation in adulthood. However, Lep deficiency in early life led to irreversible obesity via suppression of energy expenditure. Lep reactivation in adulthood led to persistent reduction in hypothalamic Pomc, Cart, and Prlh expression and defects in reproductive and brain development. The findings revealed that early defects in leptin signaling cause permanent metabolic, neuroendocrine, and developmental problems. Lept regulates energy balance and exhibits neurotrophic effects during critical periods, but the role of lept during development is not fully understood. Changes in lept signaling during development may affect organization of neural circuits that regulate energy homeostasis, favoring incidence of metabolic diseases later life. Lept may be involved in developmental programming. Early postnatal lept block in rats leads to long-term lept resistance and susceptibility to diet-induced obesity. The importance of lept signaling during development for long energy homeostasis and aspects regulated by lept is not fully understood. Lept or Lep deficiency leads reduced brain mass, disruption of projections from AR neurons to paraventric nucleus of hypothalamus, as well as behavioral and cognitive problems. The study evaluated consequences of lack of leptin in early on brain development. Lept affects developmental processes, especially in brain. Rodents exhibit postnatal lept surge between first and second age not associated with changes fat or food intake. Findings contribute to understanding of metabolic programming of metabolic, neuroocrine, and developmental consequences from absence lept in life, especially context of alarming childhood obesity worldwide. Mutations in lept or Lep impair capacity of hypothalamus to regulate homeostasis, leading to metabolic dysfunctions in humans and rodents, including obesity, hyperphag, low expenditure, activity, insulin resistance, and infertility.

Researchers investigated the Arabidopsis circadian clock at the single cell level, examining its robustness and coordination across the plant. They used a reporter for the core gene CIRIAN ASSOCIATED 1 (CA) to study the dynamics of the clock across the whole plant over several days. The data revealed robust single oscillations, albeit desynchronised, with two waves of clock activity, one going down and one up the root. Cell-to-cell coupling was observed, especially in the root, and a simple model showed that cell-to-cell coupling and period differences can generate the observed waves. The results suggest a decentralised model of clock coordination in plants, unlike the centralised mammalian clock.

The study found that damping at the plant level is mainly due to desynchronisation of oscillating cells with different periods, not noise in gene expression due to low molecule numbers or damping. Waves of gene expression were observed, one up and down the root, with variable periods at each position in the root causing desynchron. A hierarchical structure for the plant clock has been proposed, similar to mammalian, where shoot drives rhythms in root, but tissue-dependent differences must be explained. Experiments have shown waves of clock expression in leaves and striped patterns in roots.

Single cell measurements revealed tissue differences in phases and robustness of the clock, not restricted to one type, suggesting cells respond based on position. The circadian controls expression throughout day and night in organisms, a core circuit generating rhythm has been elucidated and oscillates in cells. In multi-cell organisms, single rhythms integrated for coordinated response to environment. Mechanistic models at single-level require reliable methods to measure rhythmic physiologically representative cells in isolation and experimentally test under range conditions and developmental stages.

The study observed robust in hypocotyl peak before cotyled and top root, with wave clock expression down root, fitting proposed hierarchical structure where shoot drives root. Mammals achieve by oscillations peripheral from central pacemaker brain suprachiasmatic nucleus. Arabid generates 24hr in key processes, stomata, photosynthesis, hypocot elongation, heterogeneous oscill could synchronised light-dark. Single approach consistent weak across whole but regions strong local between cells, sufficient drive increase synchron time. A model described oscillators with different periods informed data, periods faster shoot and root than rest root.

They found that multi-talker intelligibility varied widely in normal adults and could be predicted from neural-lock to modulation (FM) cues measured by ear EEG recordings. Combining neural fine processing, pupil-indexed listening, and FM accounted for 78% of variability in multialker intelligibility. Disordered bottom-up and top-down markers of poor multialker perception identified could inform next-generation clinical tests for hearing disorders.

A battery of behavioral and physiological tests accounted for nearly 80% of variability in multialker intelligibility without linguistic cues. Individuals with clinically normal hearing exhibited considerable variability in measures, but neither had meaningful relationship with multialker recognition thresholds. Pupil functions correlated with difficulty in multialker task, leading to significant correlation between pupil change and intelligibility (r=0.53, p=0.03). Researchers have developed a novel imaging technique to noninvasively measure single-cell blood flow in the smallest to largest vessels in the living mouse retina, a part of the central nervous system. Using adaptive optics and an ultrafast camera, they captured blood cells without contrast agents, enabling high-throughput measurement of hundreds of thousands of cells per second. This approach will be applicable to clinical approaches using the same instrumentation. The study measured flow in 123 vessels of 19 mice, ranging from 3.2-45.8 micrometers in diameter, providing a functional map of the vascular network in its native environment. Mean velocity was observed to be heterogeneous across vessel diameters, with arterioles, venules, and capillaries having different rates. The noninvasive nature and safe infrared light used offers possibility to measure full blood flow in humans. The study provides the first measurement of cross-sectional blood profile and absolute pulsatile velocity in the mouse retina, as well as mean and pulsatile flow in medium and small vessels. This opens new possibilities to study dynamics in full retinal sizes, enabling laminar flow in larger vessels and non-Newtonian blood rheology at capillary level. The direct measurement is critical as diameter-resistance models poorly represent particulate flow.

Researchers have developed a new software toolkit called DeepKit for estimating animal posture using deep learning models. The toolkit, called StackedNet, achieves fast and accurate pose estimation without sacrificing generalizability. StackedNet is a multi-scale deep-learning model that uses a fast GPU-based peak-detection algorithm to estimate keypoint locations with sub-pixel precision. This improves processing speed by over 2x with no loss in accuracy compared to current methods. The toolkit is demonstrated with challenging pose estimation tasks in laboratory and field settings, including groups of interacting individuals. DeepKit reduces barriers to using advanced tools for measuring behavior and has broad applicability across the sciences.

 The software is built on state-of-the-art individual estimation using convolutional networks to achieve fast inference without reducing accuracy or generalizability. The pose model, Stacked, offers improvements over models from Mathis et al. The code, documentation, and examples are available at https://github.com/javingeepose under a permissive license. To achieve fast pose estimation by Pereira et while maintaining predictive power of models like DeepCut Math, these advances could lead to larger, higher-resolution, reliable datasets revolutionizing understanding between behavior, brain, and environment.

 Stacked model can run at 30-110 Hz, depending on resolution of predicted maps. In contrast, computer vision methods, deep-learning-based can achieve near-human accuracy by annotating data and training a general-purpose algorithm to estimate locations directly from images. Two fast estimation models extend state-the model for individual estimation by New et al and current for convolution regression Jég. Models use fewer parameters than DeepCut and LE while removing limitations. These measure posture by transforming images into probabilistic key locations, confidence maps describing body for one or individuals. Confidence maps are processed to produce 2-D coordinates, used for analysis.

Deep models need large data, Math et highlighted considerations using CNN for pose and reviewed progress fully convolution models. Advancements driven by adding connections to increase performance, e.g., Jég. Future progress may require loss functions, increasing cost and complexity, manual annotating data from every view. Limitations make fundamentally different methods required. New methods replace annotations with articulated volumetric 3-D models, LEAP, attempting to limit complexity and parameterization for speed. However, comparisons suggest only limited success compared to DeepCut.

This is mediated by a drop in NAD levels and induction of mitochondrial ROS, which can be rescued by addition of products that restore NAD production. This signal is also important for host response to intracellular pathogen Salmonella typhimur, which disrupts host glucose metabolism by uptake. Researchers have identified a novel DNA-binding site in the PAL2 protein, a tumor suppressor linked to breast cancer and Fanconi anemia, and demonstrated its critical role in homologous-directed repair (HDR) in cells. Moreover, NDB stimulates inverse strand exchange and uses RNA substrates. Data reveal PAL2's versatile interaction with DNA and highlight its importance in chromosome repair.

Both BR1 N-terminal and BR2-interacting WD C-terminal domains are retained in this mutant, pointing to DB's importance in PAL2 function.

PAL is essential mammalian linked to cancers and Fan anemia. PAL C-terminal WD domain interacts BR2 while N-terminus forms complex BR1. PAL shares structural and DNA features with Rec/RAD and Rad proteins and supports strand reaction.
