Researchers investigated the brain regions and cells responsible for opioid-induced respiratory depression, a major cause of opioid overdose deaths in the US. They found that two brainstem areas, the pre-Bötzinger Complex (preBöt) and the Parabrachial Nucleus (PBNF), mediate this effect, with the preöt being the primary site. Using genetic tools, they identified 70-140 neurons in the preöt responsible for sensitivity to opioids, which can be targeted to prevent respiratory depression without affecting analgesia. Opioids act by silencing presynaptic release, removing these neurons from the network. The µ-opi receptor is expressed in both central and peripheral nervous systems, including sites that modulate breathing such as the cortex, brainstem control centers, primary neurons, and sensing afferents. Deleting µ-opi receptor from both sites eliminates opioid-induced respiratory depression, even at high doses of fentanyl. Targeting these two sites is sufficient to rescue respiratory depression.
Researchers have identified sodium taurochotransport polypeptide (NTCP) as a functional receptor for human hepatitis B virus (HBV) and hepatitis D (HDV) viruses. Using a combination of biochemical and virological assays, they found that NT specifically interacts with the pre1 domain of the HB envelope protein, a key determinant for entry of both viruses. NT is a transmembrane transporter predominantly expressed in the liver, and silencing it inhibited HBV and HDV infection, while exogenous NT expression rendered nonsusceptible hepatarcinoma cells susceptible to these viral infections. Replacing amino acids 157-165 of monkey NT with human counterpart conferred ability to support both viral infections. The study demonstrates NT is a receptor for HBV and HD. NT localizes to the sinusoidal basolateral plasma membrane of hepatocytes, a location fitting its role as receptor for bloodborne HB and HD. The discovery of NT as entry receptor for HB and HD raises questions regarding its involvement in viral pathogenesis and may lead to new prevention and treatment strategies against these viruses and related diseases.

Researchers investigated the role of the activity-regulated non-canonical Ras-like GTPase Rem2 in visual circuit plasticity. They found that Rem2- mice fail to exhibit normal ocular dominance plasticity during the critical period. At the cellular level, data establish a cell-autonomous role for Rem in regulating excitability of layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons, prior to changes in synaptic function. Consistent with these findings, in vitro and in vivo recordings reveal increased firing in the absence of Rem2. Rem functions at the nexus of a signaling network that senses and responds to changes in activity to preserve circuit function in the face of changing experience. The data demonstrate that Rem is a key molecule that regulates excitability and circuit in the context of sensory experience. Rem expression is acutely regulated by visual experience, suggesting it may play a role in regulating responsiveness of the cortex to stimulation through regulation of excitability or synaptic scaling. The study found that Rem functions as a regulator of experience-dependent plasticity at morphological, cellular, and circuit levels, providing a step in connecting molecular regulators of morphology with broader function.
The article discusses the limitations of magnetogenetics, a field that aims to make cells responsive to magnetic fields for controlling their activity, and magnetosensation, the ability of organisms to sense the Earth's magnetic field for navigation. It argues that recent claims of magnetic effects on biological molecules and cells conflict with basic laws of physics, specifically the paramagnetic nature of ferritin protein limits its utility for magnetically sensitive cells. Ferritin is a protein with 24 subunits forming a spherical shell, 12 nm in diameter, with no permanent magnetization, strictly paramagnetic or superparamagnetic at room temperature. Magnetic fields interact weakly with biological matter, reason for whole-body imaging and magnetogen appeal, but require special mechanisms to sense weak fields like the Earth's. Proposed mechanisms for magnetos and magnetogen rely on ferritin's anisotropy, torque, heating, or collective stress on channels. Calculations show none of these biophysical schemes are plausible, eliminating additional proposals along the way. Ferritin can be pulled with high fields, exploited for separation, but not suitable for non-invasive control. A permanent magnetic particle, like magnetosomes, could exert larger forces, torques, and temperatures, offering a realistic route to magnetogen.

Loss of Prosper prevents entry into quies, whereas a pulse low level of nuclear Prosper drives proliferating larval neuroblasts into quies. The researchers propose that Prosper levels distinguish three progenitor fates: absent for self-renew, low for quies, and for differentiation. Transient low nuclearo is sufficient to induce neuroblast quies. A pulse of Prosper precisely matched the timing of neuroblast quies, occurring earlier in pd mutants and later in cas mutants. Prosper is unique among basal fate determinants regulating neuroblast quies, with loss of function in numb or brat or Prosper target string showing no effect on timing. Prosper is required for neuroblast quies, as shown by quantifying number of Ed+ neurobl in prosper null embryos. High levels of Prosper trigger differentiation, whereas low levels induce quies in Dila neuroblasts. High vertebrate ortholog Pro1 also triggers differentiation, whereas low levels induce quies. Transient overexpression of nuclearo in neurobl results in quies or differentiation.
Researchers have developed a single-molecule fluorescence microscopy method to study the kinetics of HIV-1 capsid unco, the process by which the viral capsid disassembles to release the viral DNA for nuclear import. They observed that the opening of the first defect in the lattice is the rate-limiting step of unco, followed by rapid collapse. The caps-binding PF74 accelerates caps opening but stabilizes the lattice, while binding to a polyan to a conserved arginine cluster in the lattice delays initiation of unco but does not prevent lattice disassembly. The study reveals that different stages of unco can be controlled by the interplay between caps-binding regulators, determining overall unating kinetics.

The researchers measured single-particle unco kinetics of authentic HIVids after removing the viral membrane barrier, observing effects of capsid molecules on discrete steps in unco. Caps-binding exhibit differential effects on initiation and continuation of unco, with structural heterogeneity of the caps described as conical, tubular, and other shapes containing defects. They developed an in vitro imaging assay to follow unco kinetics of hundreds of HIVids in a single experiment, resolving intermediates in disassembly averaged in traditional assays. Observing properties of individualids classified them according to unating behaviors.

The majority of capsids contain defects and uncoat immediately after membrane removal, with unating an all-or process in absence of binders. The half-life of caps is long enough to use permeabilized particles as a platform to quantify binding labeled proteins to viralids at single level. The researchers used caps opening assay to characterize stability of HIVid in absence and presence of molecules affecting the lattice. The fraction leaky caps constituted 60-80% of particles, irrespective of binders present, with PF74 uncoupling opening from lattice disassembly.

The study shows measuring release from viralids at single level suitable to reveal heterogeneity assembly and effect bind on kinetics intactids. Researchers investigated the role of the JUN-terminal kinase (J) signaling pathway in breast cancer development. They found that loss of J signaling in the mammary epithelium caused cancer and accelerated tumor formation in a mouse model of breast. This was associated with widespread neoplas and genomic instability. The J pathway was shown to play a key role in tumor initiation, not established tumors. Frequent loss-of-function mutations in J pathway components were found in human breast cancer, suggesting they are 'driver mutations that promote development. The significance of J pathway inactivation in breast is unclear, but this study demonstrates its tumor-suppressive function. Researchers have identified peptidglycine-amating monxygenase (PAM) as a crucial enzyme required for the assembly of cilia in multiple cell types, including Chlamdomonas reinii, planaria, and mammalian cells. P is involved in the biogenesis of motile cilia in C. reinii and the ventral epithelium of planaria, as well as the assembly of primary cilia in neuroepithelial cells. In Chlamdomonas P knockdown lines failed to assemble cilia beyond the transition zone, had abnormal Golgi architecture, and altered levels of cilia components. Decreased P gene expression reduced motile ciliary density on the ventral surface of planaria, resulting in the appearance of cytoplasmic axemes lacking ciliary membrane. The primary cilia on neuroepith cells in Pam- mouse embryos were also aberrant. P plays a conserved role in the early steps of ciliogenesis, revealing a novel link between amidation and cilium in multiple cell types. The amid and membrane roles of P contribute to observed cili defects. P is present in the Golgi and cilia, and associated with the axem superstructure in C. reinii. In planaria, P knockdown resulted in disrupted microtule extension from the basal body, similar to IFT and I46 mutants. The bifunctional P protein in planaria has a transmembrane and cytos domain, a PH protein, and a PAL. PH activity is required for cili in planaria, while PH and P are involved in cogenesis. The cytos domain of mammalian P is not required for activity, but controls endytic trafficking and acts as a hub for cytoplasmic interactions. The location and trafficking of cili factors in the presence and absence of P are illustrated. P catalytic domains are in the lumen of secretory pathway, acting on glyc-extended peptides and lipids to yield amid products. In the cil, P domains are exposed to the extracellular environment.

To understand how these channels activate at different voltages, researchers combined modeling, mutagenesis, and electrophysiology to analyze the structures, energy, and kinetics of two functionally distinct voltage-sensing domains (VSD) of the eukotic calcium channel Ca1.1. Both SDs displayed features of the sliding helix model, but differed in ion-pair formation of outer charges. Stabilization of the activated state enhanced voltage of activation, while stabilization of resting states slowed kinetics. This mechanism explains how ionair formation in SDs can realize characteristic properties of voltageated cation channels.

Researchers investigated the role of matrix metalloproteinases (MMPs) in adult visual plasticity, specifically in healthy and stroke-affected brains. They found that optimal MMP activity is crucial for visual plasticity to occur, and that upregulation of MMP after stroke compromises experience-induced plasticity, which can be rescued by brief MMP inhibition. In healthy mice, 7-day application of MMP-inhib prevented plasticity, while treatment with MMP-inhib once but not twice after stroke rescued plastic normally lost under conditions. Data suggest a critical role of MMPs in adult plasticity, with upregulation shortly after cortical lesion impairing experience visual plastic, which can be rescued by brief inhibition. Precise regulation of MMPactivity seems essential for plastic in the adult brain, offering opportunities for treatment and recovery after stroke. A single treatment after stroke rescued plastic in both paradigms, whereas treatment twice diminished plastic-promoting effect. Together, data reveal a crucial role of MMPs in adult plastic and suggest MMPactivity within a narrow window for experience plastic to occur.
Researchers used bifunctional methaniosulfate cross-linkers to investigate the conformation of AMPA receptors in the presence and absence of the auxiliary subunit Stargin. Their data indicate that the extracellular layer of AMP receptors can get trapped in stable, opened conformations, especially upon long exposures to glutamate. In contrast, Stargin limits this conformation flexibility. Under synaptic conditions, where glutamate and the presence of auxiliary proteins, the extracellular domains of AMP receptors likely stay compact during gating. This suggests that extracellular domains of synaptic AMP receptors are unlikely to undergo large rearrangements during transmission and work in a compact conformation regime unless faced with long exposures to glutamate in pathological conditions. Researchers have developed an optimized protocol for isolating immune cells from the intestines of mice infected with helmin, a type of parasitic worm, which has been challenging due to strong anti-parasite type 2 immune responses that drive mucus production, tissue remodeling, and immune cell infiltration. They used a systematic approach to optimize the standard intestinal digestion protocol and successfully isolated millions of immune cells from the infected duodenum. To validate the accuracy of these cells, they analyzed them using high-dimensional flow cytometry and confirmed findings with confocal microscopy. The isolation and high-dimensional analysis allowed them to identify hallmarks of anti-parite immune responses throughout the course of helmin infection and has potential to accelerate single-cell discoveries of local helmin immune responses previously unfeasible.

 The cells were characterized using a 23-color flow cytometry panel incorporating hallmark and intracellular markers for type 2 immune responses associated with helmin infections. The isolation and high-dimensional analysis enabled characterization of innate and adaptive-parite immune responses throughout the course of helmin infection, validated with confocal microscopy and observed differences in cell ratios between naïve and infected samples reported by both approaches. Importantly, many changes were observed locally, highlighting the need for good cell techniques to investigate intestinal responses against helmin directly.

The researchers also extracted high-quality RNA from isolated cells, which could accelerate single-cell discoveries of helmin immune through single-cell analysis, such as single-cell sequencing, previously unfeasible. They modified EDTA wash steps to remove mucus, but observed no improvement in yield, followed by testing various collagenases for intestinal digestion, hypothesizing remodeling during helmin infection impacted procedure. Collagen A-based digestion and modified wash increased yield, and strong vortexing after wash improved outcome, suggesting epithelium harder to remove in helmin-infected tissues.

The optimized protocol was used to investigate acute and chronic helmin infection in C57/6 mice with Heligomosoides polyrus baker, a naturally occurring rodent parasite with intestinal cycle. Duodenum mesenteric lymph nodes were digested with Liberase and DNase and passed through 70µm strainer for flow cytometry cells resuspended in 0.05 DNase. Compared to naïve animals, both standard and optimized protocols resulted in 3-6 million live cells duod with 70-80 viability and 20-30 frequency CD+ cells. Samples from all points digested using optimized protocol, duod from 14-28 days post yielded 3-6 million cells, with doubling to 8-11 million at 7 days post and similar CD+ in crysections.
Researchers have developed a comprehensive mathematical model to describe the dynamics of the 20S proteasome, a key enzyme responsible for protein degradation in the cell. The model combines experimental and computational approaches to understand the interactions between substrates and products and proteolytic sites. The study reveals that the rate-limiting step of hydrolysis is the transport of substrates into the proteome, with efficiency varying between standard and immuno-proteomes affecting total degradation and cleavage usage. 

 The model describes core proteome features that reflect proteome dynamics in complex scenarios, such as inside the cellular environment. Polyubiquit tagging regulates protein half-life and affects metabolic processes. Despite biochemical efforts, mechanisms of proteomal action and differences in substrate proteysis between proteome isoforms are incompletely understood. The 20 proteome is dynamic, catalytic activity varying over time due to interactions with substrates and regulatory sites.

 The model provides an interpretative framework for proteas action, accounting for substrate-specific effects. It helps understand degradation of specific proteins in cells and forms of proteome, such as bound to regulatory complexes 19 and 28. The study focuses on dynamics of exemplar peptides, regulated by distinct mechanisms, and elucidates steps in proteomal that cannot be probed through experiments. Substrate transport into proteome cavities is a key in regulation, despite being neglected in analysis.

 The integrative analysis suggests immuno-proteome has higher affinity for non-catal modifier activated substrates, located at the inner surface. The model is a step towards understanding degradation proteomes. Interactions between substrates and proteomal machinery shape degradation rates, influencing processes like signal transduction. Steps in proteolysis may be isoform-specific: transport, binding, hydrolysis, and release. Different kinetics of substrates by s and i-proas or i-proas are involved in pathologies, with i-proome deficient mice altered proteome kinetics.

 The model incorporates structural features into proteome action, consisting of stacked rings, with catalytic sites. s-proas have stronger affinity for ZLECA than i-proas, explained by β1 and βi catal pockets. The model predicts i-proas have higher affinity, hydrolysis, and cooperativity than s-proas. The proteome cavity is filled by peptides, facilitating other molecules until effective volume is filled, with substrate and product numbers close to maxima.
Researchers have identified a new β-alanine transporter, named Bala, which plays a crucial role in visual transmission in Drosila. β-alanine recycling is essential for sustained synaptic transmission, and histamine is rapidly taken up by laminar glia, converted to carcinine, and delivered back to photoreceptors, where it is hydrolyzed to histamine and βanine. Bala is a plasma membrane transporter capable of transporting β-al into cells. Mutations in the Bala gene disrupted photore synaptic, photaxis, and retinal β-al distribution. Researchers have discovered that chronologically aging yeast cells undergo a sudden redox collapse, where almost 80% of identified thiol-containing proteins are oxidized before cell death. Contrary to the free radical theory of aging, this collapse is not triggered by an increase in endogenous oxidants, but by a drop in NAD levels, which normally provides electrons for cellular redox homeostasis. This decrease in NAD occurs early in lifespan and sets off a cascade that downregulates cellular processes. Caloric restriction, a universal lifespan-extending measure, increases NAD and delays each facet of the cascade. Studies reveal a timeline of events leading to system-wide oxidation of the proteome before cell death.

The researchers used chronologically aging S. cerevisiae to determine onset, extent, and targets of protein oxidation in postmit aging cells. By monitoring thiol status of 300 proteins, they found cells undergo global red collapse leading to thiol oxidation in 80% of proteins days before death. They also uncovered protein targets whose oxidative thiol modifications contribute to physiological alterations in aging. Chronological lifespan S. cerevisiae represents a model for aging postmit cells.

Results suggest early changes in NAD levels trigger initial oxidation of thioreductase and changes in cellular red potential, leading to red collapse in postmit yeast. Cultivation under caloric conditions delays decrease in NAD, delays red collapse of proteome, and extends lifespan. Researchers used quantitative proteomic approach to assess redox events in non-dividing cells, chronologically S. cerevisiae as model. Downregulation of translation contributes to oxidative stress and extends lifespan in replicative yeast and other organisms.

Caloric restriction increases chronological lifespan in yeast, significantly. Molecular mechanism not elucidated, but trait among calorically restricted is increased stress. Study used chronologically S. It is tempting to speculate reversible thiol in chron aging cells represent 'pro' response to protect proteins against damage and extend lifespan. Analysis GSH/GSS ratio in chron aging cells, dependent on NPH glutathione reductase, prooxid shift coincided with decrease N levels. Before prooxid shift delayed in calorically growth. Importantly, oxidation delayed caloric and hypometabolic conditions suggest maintenance red homeostasis contribute extending regimens.
The study presents the mechanistic basis of D-aminoacyl-tRNA deacylase (DTD) from Plasmodium falcum, an enzyme responsible for removing D-amino acids from tRNAs to prevent their incorporation into proteins. The crystal structure of DTD with a substrate-mim analog reveals an invariant crossunit Glyis dipeptide captures the chiral center of D-amacylRNA. No protein residues are directly involved in catalysis, but the unique side-independent substrate recognition explains D's ability to act on multiple-am acids. The chiral specificity ensures L-amacyl-tRNAs escape proofreading. The study provides insights into fundamental enantio process and elucidates chiral mechanism crucial in preventing-am acid incorporation during translation.

 DTD's side-free recognition provides basis for nature's design of single deacy to deal with D-tRNA and role of weak bonds in chirality selection. An L- would bind in one of three possible conformations. The study corrects earlier mechanistic models based on docking approaches or structures with non-cognate ligands. D acts as freanding modules, likely operating by recapturing aa-tRNAs from EFu. Amino acid site captures chiral C and crossunit interactions, particularly Glyis motif, plays central role in recognition.

The mechanism rules out other D binding modes, as orientation and position fixed by terminal adenos. The structure of proof domains with substrate-mim analogs shows rejection. The rejection also explains stringent specificity to avoid depletion of L pool. Biochemical studies indicated configurational preference, but mechan basis remained elusive due to lack of cognate-bound structure. The crystal of D from E. coli delineates catalytic mechanism, looking at residues near susceptible bond between adenos and carbonyl of Drosine. The site and orientation are variable, with flexibility around torsion C-C bond giving three possibilities.

The study investigates the mechanism of spontaneous opening of the cystic fibrosis transmembrane conductance regulator (CFTR) channel, a chloride channel mutated in cystic fibrosis (CF) patients, in the absence of ATP. It shows that spontaneous openings are strictly coupled to the dimerization of the cytosolic nucleotide binding domains (NBDs) of CFTR, which may occur even without ATP hydrolysis. The study uses thermodynamic mutant cycle formalism to correlate energetic changes in coupling residues across the dimer interface with pore opening/closure in single CF channels. The results suggest that spontaneous openings are linked to NBD dimer formation, similar to the presence of bound ATP. This constrains mechanistic models of ATP-dependent gating and understanding the driving forces, likely conserved among ABC proteins. The study also explains modulatory effects of mutations and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity, providing insights for understanding CF and drug mechanisms. The findings have implications for understanding the impact of phosphorylation, mutations, and drugs on ATP-driven and spontaneous activity.
Researchers have proposed a new mechanism for the assembly of transmembrane proteins in lipid bilayers, referred to as the "pre-transition" or "orderobic" effect. This effect arises from the first-order transition between ordered and disordered phases in the membrane. Using large-scale molecular simulations, they show that a protein with hydrophobic thickness equal to the disordered phase embedded in an ordered bilayer stabilizes a microscopic–orderdis interface. The stiffness of this interface is finite. When such proteins approach each other, they assemble by reducing the interfacial energy. This phenomenon is analogous to the hydrophobic effect and is referred to as the "orderobic" effect. The effect is mediated by proximity to the order-dis phase and hydroph mismatch of the protein. The strength and range of forces from this effect are significantly larger than those from membrane elasticity for considered.

 The orderobic effect depends on the ability of the protein to induce disordered in its vicinity, which depends on proximity to the membrane's transition and protein's hydroph mismatch. For instance, a transmembrane with hydroobic thickness 2.6nm favors the disordered phase. If large enough, it can melt the ordered near and form an order-dis interface. The prediction of lipid surrounding orderobic proteins may be tested by single-molecule techniques. Biological transmem proteins are more complicated than models, with multiple components allowing more than one order-dis transition.

 The orderobic has a dual effect: a transmem in dis that favors ordered can nucleate an ordered and order-dis interface. For example, a protein with positive mismatch induces in its vicinity. Interfaces separating ordered and dis regions provide a force for assembly. A consequence of regular packing is hydroobic thickness of ordered larger than dis. The area per lipid in ordered is smaller than dis. The orderobic drives proteins from ordered to dis and creates dis as a result of clustering. Both effects have been noted in simulations of α-helix in coexisting liquid-ordered and dis domains.

 The orderobic is a pre-effect for the first transition between ordered and dis phases, occurring in dis membranes close to coexistence between liquid-ordered and dis. It may be relevant in lungurfant monolayers. Proteins with zero mismatch do not induce dis and the effect vanishes. The orderobic produces excess mobility by melting a layer and facilitating motions. This explains protein reorganization in 'gel phases. Results are applicable to monolayers governing diffusion and assembly within rigid phases. The orderobic plays roles in fusion, budding, and signaling.
Researchers investigated the relationship between action potentials (APs) and fluorescence signals in genetically encoded calcium indicators (GECs) in transgenic mice. They characterized the AP-fluorescence transfer function in vivo for layer 2/3 pyramidal neurons in primary visual cortex with simultaneous imaging and cell-attached recordings from mice expressing GCMP6 or GCMPf. Most APs were detected under optimal conditions, but only a minority of 1 and 2 AP events were detected (<10% and ~20, respectively) under typical population imaging conditions, emphasizing the limits of AP detection. The study provides a ground truth dataset from anesthetized mice with simultaneous electrophiology and imaging, revealing that only a small fraction of isolated APs were detected under population conditions with existing inference algorithms. The results indicate that even GCMP indicators are bright enough to detect most 1 AP in superficial pyram neurons if detection is optimized, but most events containing 1, 2, and greater AP go undet in imaging with GC6. The study contributes to understanding the link between calcium-evoked imaging and spiking activity, making the dataset available for the community.
Researchers have discovered a unique biological valve in the female genitalia of the Brazilian insect Neotla that enables females to receive multiple seminal packages from the same or different males during copulation. The valve is composed of two plate-like sclerites, a closure element, and in-flow canals, and allows females to control the direction of seminal flow. Females have a penis-like introment organ to anchor males and obtain voluminous semen, which is packed in a capsule formed by seminal injection. The capsule is not only used for fertilization but also consumed by the female as nutrition. The complex has two slots for insemination, enabling Neotla to mate while one slot is occupied. This valve is a morphological novelty enabling females to compete for seminal in nutrient-poor habitats through long copulations and multiple injections. The evolution of this valve may have been a prerequisite for the reversal of introment organ in Neotla.

The male-holding organ (spine female) and long copulations of 40-70 hours compared to 50 minutes for one capsule in relatives Neotla (Yoshawa et al, 2014) can be considered adaptations for escalated competition for nial gifts in this group of insects inhabiting oligot habitats (Lien and Ferreira 201; Lien and Ferreira 201; Yoshawa et al 201). This enables to receive additional from same or males using empty.

Researchers investigated the role of Munc-18 in the assembly of SNARE proteins, which are crucial for synaptic vesicle exocytosis. M-18 initiates SN assembly and stabilizes the half-zip SN complex, independent of syntax N-terminal regulatory domain (NRD). The study demonstrates distinct functions of SN domains in assembly, which is chapered by M-18.

The most M18 activity is to enhance de novo SN assembly, observed by unfolding and relaxing the complex to a low force to detect reassembly. M18 induces T folding and stabilizes SN to facilitate assembly and fusion, similar to V and V peptides. The study corroborates M18 enhances trans SN complexes, promoting liposome-lip fusion.

Researchers have identified a new electrophysiological marker that tracks states of reduced arousal in humans, including sleep and anesthesia. The marker is the 1/f slope of the electrophys power, which reflects the non-oscillatory, scale component of neural activity. This marker discriminates wakefulness from propof anesthesia, NREM sleep, and REM sleep based on the electrophys brain state. The study used intracran and surface recordings in four independent datasets and found that the slope becomes more negative (power steeper) in sleep and anesthesia compared to wakefulness. The slope also discriminates wake from NREM and REM sleep solely based on electroph brain state. The findings provide a common electroph marker that tracks states of reduced arousal, including sleep and anesthesia in humans. The marker is observable on a wide range of timescales from sub-epochs to full recordings. The study also found that the slope mirrors rapid changes in excitability over the course of a slow wave, providing a mechanistic link between the slope and synchrony during oscillations. The electroph features of NREM stage 3 (slow sleep) and propof anesthesia are similar, with an increase in low frequency and occurrence of slow oscillations.
Researchers have investigated the role of microtubules (MTs) and actin filaments (AFs) in the formation and movement of stromules, dynamic tubular extensions of chloroplasts that connect with nuclei during innate immunity. They found that MTs are required for stromule extension and movement, with depolymerization leading to retraction and stabilization increasing frequency. AFs are not necessary for extension but provide anchor points for stromules, guiding chloroplast movement towards the nucleus. A correlation between strom direction and chloroplast movement was observed, suggesting a general function of stromules in epidermal cells. The study proposes a model where stromules extend along MTs and attach to AF around nuclei, facilitating movement of chloroplasts to nuclei during innate immunity. Results show that MT and AF may facilitate peruclear clustering of chloroplasts during an immune response.

The study analyzed the mechanism of strom extension and movement in chloroplast of green tissue and peruclear chlor clustering during innate immunity. MTs are required for strom extension, with depolymerization leading to retraction and stabilization. AFs provide anchor points for stromules, guiding chloroplast movement towards the nucleus. Strom-directed chloroplast movement was observed in steady-state conditions without immune induction, indicating a general function of stromules in epider cells. The results show that MT and AF may facilitate peruclear clustering of chloroplast during innate response.

The cytoskeleton supports various cellular functions, including cytoplasm streaming, cell division, elongation, growth, ves trafficking, nuclear positioning, and morphogenesis. In this study, stromules extend along MT and AF stabilize chloroplast-nuclear connections during immune response. Results show that MT-mediated strom extension and AF-mediated anchoring are complementary during strom formation and movement. The study provides mechanistic insights into interactions with the cytoskeleton and stabilize stromules, describing a new type of organellar movement along MT that is strom-directed and a mechanism for peruclear during immunity.

Stromules have the ability to direct chloroplast movement and AF anchoring strom may guide peruclear clustering during immunity. Previous studies in non-green hypocotyl indicated a role for AF and MT during strom formation. The results invoke a model where, during effector-triggered immunity, MT facilitate strom extensions and strom bind to AF around nuclei. The role of MT during formation of strom-nucle connections requires further studies, but data suggests once formed, strom may guide or pull chloroplast towards nucleus, resulting in peruclear clustering.
Researchers have developed a method to predict residue-residue interactions across protein interfaces using evolutionary information. They used a pseudo-likelihood method to identify pairs of amino acids that covary across protein-protein interfaces in the 50S ribosomal subunit and 28 bacterial complexes with known structures. The results showed that these pairs are almost always in contact in the complex, provided the number of aligned sequences is greater than the average length of the proteins. This method was used to predict subunit contacts for 36 complexes with unknown structures and models for four complexes, including the tripart ATP-independent periplasm (TR) transporter, tripart efflux system, pyruvate form lyase-activating enzyme, and methine ABC transporter.

The study found that residue-residue covariance occurs frequently between physically interacting pairs but rarely between non-interacting pairs, making it useful for predicting protein interactions. The method was applied to a set of 41 protein pairs with known structures, generating models within 2Å of the native structure in 38 cases and within 4Å in one case. For the 50S ribosome, the method used sequence from ~1500 non-redund genomes, suggesting that with large numbers of aligned, residue-residue interactions can be predicted with high confidence based on amino sequence covariance.

A docking protocol was developed using predicted contacts as restraints and sampled space to generate models of protein-protein complexes. The best model had an interface within 4Å of native in 14 cases and more than half native contacts in 16. Residue covariance in complexes with structures showed that pairs with high GREIN scores are almost always in contact in experimentally determined structures. The method was used to predict contacts for 36 complexes with unknown structures and models for four complexes, including the tripart ATP-independent perasm (TR) transporter, tripart efflux system, pyv formate lyase-activ enzyme, and methine ABC.

The results demonstrate strong selective pressure at protein-protein interfaces beyond residue conservation and co-evolving pairs are nearly in contact in protein. Not all residues across interfaces co-evolve nor protein-protein interfaces. The study focused on prokaryotes and mitochondria, but the approach is applicable to any taxa.
Researchers investigated how humans categorize visual scenes by accumulating information from multiple locations in a scene using a novel gaze-contingent paradigm in a categorization task. Participants' eye movements followed an active strategy that incorporated prior knowledge of the scene and statistical structure of patterns. Categorization performance improved when locations were revealed by an optimal Bayesian sensor. A combination of Bayesian observer and sensor estimated that 70% of apparent optimality in fixation arose from biases, perceptual noise, and inaccuracies in eye movements. The process of selecting locations was 70% efficient in the task. Results suggest participants select movements to maximize information about categories requiring integration from multiple locations.

The study extends active sensing to tasks with arbitrary features, not spatial, such as categories. It provides first fixation-by-fixation analysis under eye movements matching observer to participants' performance. The task is fundamentally different from search, where a single location has necessary information, and movements are described by inhibition of return. An experiment with patterns controlled and simple ensured foveal vision dominated a gaze-conting task tracked eye and revealed small apert of image at each location. In contrast, participants used prior knowledge and accumulated scene to guide movements. A control simulated three strategies reproduced aspects of actual movements but lacked full-loop interaction between belief and eyeovement. The ideal model quantified amount of information accumulated image over revealings for different strategies. Most studies not addressed interplay between gathering and selection of natural active sensing. Our shows high-efficiency is a strategy humans adopt without deliberation, evidenced by inter-sadic intervals and reaching asymptotic efficiency in first session.
Researchers investigated the transport of large cargoes through the nuclear pore complex (NPC) in cells, focusing on the role of size and number of nuclear localization sequences (NLS) on import efficiency. They used caps-like particles with diameters of 17-36 nm and up to 240 NLS, and found that import requirements scale non-linearly with size and can be recapitulated by a two-parameter biophysical model correlating flux with energetic transport. For small cargoes, biochemical and physicochemical properties influence transport, but for large cargoes, binding to multiple Importin complexes masks surface properties. The study expands the range of sizes and NLS characterised, showing that 36 nm caps can enter the NPC but are too large to pass intact into the nucleus. The model assumes caps do not interact during transport, but cargoes with NLS bind transport receptors can rapidly enter the nucleus. Theoretical work could help understand export of large cargoes, such as pre-ribosomal subunits and RNA complexes.

Researchers used functional magnetic resonance imaging (fMRI) to investigate the ventral occipito-temporal cortex (VOT) responses to auditory and visual stimuli in sighted and congenitally blind individuals. The study aimed to determine if visual experience is necessary for the development of categorical organization in VOT. Results showed that VOT reliably encodes categories in both sight and blind individuals using a representational structure and connectivity similar to the one found in vision. However, sound categories were more reliably encoded in the blind group using a representational format closer to the one in vision. Crucially, VOT represents categorical membership of sounds rather than acoustic features. The study suggests that sounds trigger categorical responses in VOT of congenitally blind and sight people that match the topography and functional profile of visual responses, despite nuances in categorical organization between modalities.

The study demonstrates that categorical organization similar to vision can emerge in VOT in absence of visual experience and that categorical responses to sounds are enhanced and stable in congenally blind. Previous studies have shown that occipital cortex enhances response to non-visual processing in absence of vision. The study used representational analysis to show that membership predicts represent structure of sounds in VOT in blind rather than lower-level acoustic attributes. VOT encodes categorical membership of sounds from eight categories in sight and blind using topography, represent format, and connectivity similar to the observed in response to images in vision. However, the representation of auditory stimuli in VOT exhibits different nuances compared to organization in sight people.

The study used sensory non-linguistic stimuli to investigate sensory and categorical nature of representation in VOT. This study investigates the role of microRNA-9 (miR-9) in dendritic development and synaptic transmission in the brain. mi-9 is one of the most abundant miRNAs in the brain, and its function has been characterized in neural progenitors, but its role in dendritic and synaptic development is unknown. To target mi-9 in vivo, a transgenic miRNA sponge line was developed, allowing conditional inactivation of mi-9 in a spatio-temporally controlled manner. Using this approach, mi-9 was found to control dendritic growth and synaptic transmission in vivo. mi-9-mediated downregulation of transcription repressor REST is essential for proper dendritic growth. The study defines a key role for mi-9 in postmitotic differentiation of neurons and presents a double mouse line to characterize mi- activity in brain cells. Analysis of the mi-9-SNest-C mouse showed inhibition of mi- results in aberrant dendritic growth in vivo, accompanied by concomitant impairment in synaptic transmission. Researchers investigated the neural basis of social engagement difficulties in autism spectrum disorder (ASD) using resting-state functional magnetic resonance imaging (MRI) in a large sample of toddlers with ASD and non-ASD comparison groups. They found that ASD-related hypoconnectivity between the default mode network (DM) and visual and attention networks. Increased DM-OT hypoconnectivity was related to severity of social-communication difficulties, but only in Geo ASD. Early social-visual hypoconnect is a key neurobiological feature of Geo ASD and may be critical for future social-communic development and new targets for early intervention.

The study used a simple eye-tracking test to identify Geo ASD with high precision in early development. Social-visual hypoconnect is a key neurobi feature of Geo ASD and may be critical for social development. Although visual difficulties are early features of ASD, the neural basis is not well understood. The study found evidence for atypical functional brain organization in ASD involving social circuitry relevant to visual engagement. The DM is functionally disconnected from visual cortices and attention in the first 4 years of life. This is important for why social difficulties are a symptom in ASD and shared across toddlers.

In Drosophila melanogaster, the hematopoietic organs produce two types of mature hemocytes: plasocytes and crystal cells. Plasocytes are phagocytic and functionally compared to vertebrate macrophages, while crystal cells are nonagocytic and produce proolase, an essential for melanization. During larval development, hemocyte numbers increase, but mature crystal cells do not divide, raising the question of their origin. We show that new crystal cells in third instar larvae originate through a Notch-dependent plasocyte transdifferentiation in sessile clusters. This process is contingent on the integrity of these structures and relies on structure-dependent signaling to promote homeostasis. The existence of this hematietic tissue creates a paradigm for addressing questions in Dophila hematopoies and parallels with vertebrate systems. Macrophages are the most plastic cells in vertebrate hematiet and their specialization depends on local microenvironment. Similarly, we show in Doph larvae, hemocyte microenvironment induces crystal differentiation from plasocytes through cell-contact involving Notch signaling. The study of hematiet organs and function is essential to understand mature blood cell origin and control. In Doph, embryonic hematopois produces two types of mature hem: plas and crystal. Plas are phoc and functionally compared to vertebrate macro, while crystal are nonoc and produce proase. We aimed to explain the increase of circulating and sessile crystal numbers during Doph larval development. This is a paradox: no crystal has been seen dividing during lar stages and cells in lymph do not enter circulation under homeostatic conditions. We consider this novel hematiet, attached to circulating population and relying on structure events, brings closer to addressing questions of Doph hematies.
Researchers have found that chromosomal amplification is common in wild yeast strains of Saccharomyces cerevisiae, which are naturally tolerant of aneuploidy and display a mode of dosage compensation at the transcript level for specific classes of amplified genes. Genes subject to dosage compensation are buffered against copy-number variation (CN) and show elevated rates of amplification in natural isolates. The study generated isogenic aneuploid-eup strain pairs for three wild strains and found that up to 40% of amplified genes in aneuploid strains show lower-than-expected expression. The reduced expression is due to heritable polymorphisms that downregulate expression of amplified genes, some of which may have been selected during adaptation to anupidy. Dosage compensation functions at 10-30% of amplified genes, and genes subject to dosage are higher constrained in wild populations but show elevated rates of amplification, suggesting copy-number is buffered at these genes. Aneupidy provides a clear advantage to strain Y100 by amplifying a gene that escapes compensation. The study presents a model where dosage buffers amplification through anupidy to provide a natural, likely transient route to rapid phenotypic evolution.

Researchers used photo-switchable tweezers to investigate the mechanism of pore opening of the P2X receptor, a trimeric ion channel. They engineered cysteine residues in the transmembrane domain (TMD) with photo-switchable azobenz tweez, which can be controlled by light to measure interatomic distances in the TMD during activation. Combining experimental and computational studies provided high-resolution models of the channel in open and closed states. The results show that the outer expansion of the pore is reduced compared to the ATP-bound structure, and the inner and outer ends of adjacent pore-lining helices come closer during opening, likely through a hinge-bending motion. This provides new insight into the gating of P2 receptors and establishes a versatile strategy for other membrane proteins.

Researchers have discovered a new role for the flagellar sheath, a membranous structure surrounding the flagella of some bacteria, in mediating the release of immunogenic lipopolysaccharide (LPS) in Vibrio fischer and its pathogenic congener Vibrio cholera. Using the squid-vibrio symbiosis as a model, they found that rotation of the sheathed flagella promotes the release of LPS, a potent immunogenic molecule, in both V. fischer and V. cholera. The flagellar sheath has been an enigmatic, with its function unknown, and its role in bacterial motility poorly characterized. This study reveals a new understanding of the sheath's contribution to bacterial virulence. Further work will investigate the mechanisms of squid-vrio symbiosis, including characterizing LPS released by V. fischer during flag rotation, identifying squid receptors for V. fischer LPS, and determining flagellin's role in host responses. Flagella are virulence factors facilitating host-micro interactions, mediating migration, adherence, and invasion. In V. fischer, disruption of flag rotation in V. cholera reduced LPS release, suggesting this mechanism conserved among bacteria with sheathed flagella. The flagellar she presents a conundrum: coating a spinning structure with LPS, another immunostimolecule. The biological function of she remains unknown, posited to evade immune recognition of enclosed flagin. The phenomenon of flag-mediated L release may be conserved in other animal-associated bacteria with sheathed flagella, including human pathogens Brucella melensis and Helicobacter pylori.

Researchers studied the genetic and environmental factors that influence the production of specialized metabolites in Arabidopsis thaliana, a plant species that produces diverse metabolites to cope with environmental challenges. They analyzed 797 accessions from Europe and found that geography, environment, demography, and genetics all contribute to the diversity of metabolites and their distribution. The study focused on the glucosinolate (GSL) pathway, which is responsible for producing specialized metabolites, and found that three major loci controlling GSL diversity contain multiple alleles that recreate the same phenotypes through single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and structural variation. This suggests within-species convergence, where genotypes with the same metabolic profile contain different haplotypes that arose from distinct haplotic lineages. The distribution of GSL diversity across Europe is influenced by demography and ecological factors, with the Iberian Peninsula and Mediterranean having low variability and Central/North Europe having high diversity. The study raises the possibility of parallel evolution, where new hapypes with identical metabolic consequences arise multiple times from a single haplotype.
Apoptotic caspases have long been regarded as tumor suppressors, but recent work suggests they can promote tumor growth under certain conditions. In Drosila, oncogenic mutations in the proto-oncogene RasV (Ras12) maintain cells in an 'und-like condition and convert caspases from suppressors to promoters. Instead of killing cells, casases promote the generation of intra- and extracellular reactive oxygen species (ROS). One function of ROS is the recruitment and activation of macrophage-like cells, which signal back to tumorous epithelial cells to activate oncogenic J signaling JNK. J further amplifies casase activity, constituting a feedback loop. Interfering with the loop reduces neoplastic behavior and improves organism survival. Ras12-modified casases initiate a feedback loop involving tumorous epithelial and macroage cells necessary for uncontrolled growth and invasive behavior.

 Casases induce apoptosis in a few tumor cells but largely promote ROS generation, required for growth and invasion of neoplastic cells. This extends previous models of E and J signaling from anti-tumor to pro-tumor by oncogenic Ras and casases as essential components. Apoptotic casases are considered tumor suppressors, but under conditions like oncogenic Ras12 in scri mutant, they adopt tumor-promoting role. Ras12 switches proopt activity of casases to tumor-promoting and maintains scri- Ras12 in 'und' state. Inhibition of casases blocks growth and invasion. Tumor-prom function of casases dependent on intra and extracellular ROS required for neop behavior of scri Ras12 clones.
A study investigated the relationship between social interaction and subjective time perception, finding that social interactions are perceived as shorter in duration compared to non-social interactions. The effect was stronger in individuals with lower autistic-like tendencies. Intranasal oxytocin administration restored the effect in less socially proficient individuals, while atosiban, an oxytoc antagonist, diminished it in socially proficient ones. This suggests time is not a faithful representation of physical time, but idiosyncratic and ingrained with personality. Oxytoc is involved in mediating time of social interaction, supporting its role in human cognition. Autistic traits are associated with reduced oxy levels and ameliorated with intranas oxyin administration. The link between autistic tendency and duration of interactions raises the question if oxy plays a role. To verify, socially proficient individuals were given atos, which reduced the temporal effect compared to placebo. Oxyin administration promoted the effect in less proficient. The relationship among proficiency, oxyin, and processing social stimuli suggests oxy modulates efficacy of interactions and subjective time. The study manipulated displays of agents to dissociate perception of interaction and motion, assessing in varying social proficiency and oxyin process. Oxy administration promoted the effect in less, while atos diminished in proficient. The compression can't be accounted by a dedicated module for temporal relationships, specific to social. Oxy drives motivation, giving rise to shortened duration following administration. In proficient, influence is complicated and hard to predict. Brain imaging studies indicate oxy modulates responses in ST, TPJ, and prefrontal cortex in social tasks. Alterations facilitate processing in less proficient, exogenous oxy has a prosocial effect. Proficiency varies among neurotypical, behaviorally stable trait, associated with oxy and OTR polymorphisms. The study demonstrates motive of companionship in mental representation, sequences engaging interactions perceived shorter than non-communicative. This adds to extant research on perception, primarily dopamine, serotonin, and acetylcholine. The duration is product of neuroocrine and neural processes, ingrained with social traits.
A study investigated the effects of common mutations in the SARS-CoV-2 Spike receptor binding domain (RBD) and its ligand, human angiotensin-converting enzyme 2 (ACE) on their binding affinity and kinetics. Mutations in R are present in SARS-CoV variants of concern that have emerged worldwide, such as the B.1.1.1.7 lineage with a N501Y mutation in its RBD that enhances binding to ACE. The study analyzed the effects of five RBD mutations (K417, K417, N501, E484, and 477) and two ACE mutations (19 and K26) on the R/ACE interaction. Most mutations increased the affinity of R/ACE interaction, except for K417/T, which decreased it. The study suggests that N501 and 477 mutations enhance transmission by increasing binding, K417/T facilitate immune escape, and E484 enhances both binding and escape.

Researchers have generated the largest map of genetic interactions in metazoan cells to date, comprising 136 Drosophila genes involved in cellular processes such as signalling, chromatin, and cell cycle regulation. They used combinatorial RNA interference and single-cell phenotyping to quantify 21 phenotypes in cultured cells and devised a method combining interactions on multiple phenotypes to reveal relationships. The study presents a powerful approach for reconstructing regulatory networks and provides a resource for interpreting genetic alterations.

Researchers have identified the CYP73450 gene as the locus responsible for style dimorphism in Primula primros, a plant species with heterosty controlled by the S-loc supergene. CYP50 is present on the short-styled S-morph haplotype, specifically expressed in S-m styles, and its loss leads to long styles. The gene arose via duplication in the Primulaceae lineage and shows accelerated molecular evolution. The study provides a mechanistic explanation for Primula style dimorphism and sheds light on the evolution of the Sloc as a model for a complex supergene. CYP50 is a rare example of an identified locus underlying adaptive polymorphism, opening the way for understanding the origin of the Prim Slocus as an iconic case of a super. Hetery evolved once in the genus Primula, and the causal genes should co-segregate with morphotypes in heterostous Prim species. To test CYP50, the researchers sequenced transcriptomes of distantly related P species and found the presence/absence polymorphism strictly cosegregates with morph in all heterous sampled across the Prim phylogeny. The gene is absent in all cases of longostly, supporting the conclusion that CYP50 is the locus of the Prim hetery super that determines style polymorphism.

A novel centri-associated protein, C120, plays a critical role in maintaining centrosome homeostasis by inhibiting the maturation of the daughter centri in quiescent cells. Depletion of C120 in mouse and human cells leads to accumulation of pericentolar material (PCM) components, including pericin, C5ap2, ninein, and C170, resulting in increased microtubule-nucleation at the centrosome. This causes aberrant dyin-dependent trafficking of centrosomal proteins, dispersal of centolar satellites, and defective ciliary assembly and signaling. C120 is enriched on the outer wall of the centolar microtubules and regulates the asymmetric distribution of PCM at the centome. Loss of C120 disrupts ciliary assembly and function, likely due to aberrant CS-dependent trafficking of proteins to the centome. Centri satellites play essential roles in cogenesis, and dispersal of CS upon C120 loss disrupts the formation and function of cilia in quies cells. The study highlights the importance of centome homeasis and its relationship to primary cilium and signaling, and uncovers a new molecular defect underlying the pathogenesis of ciliopathies such as Jeune and Jert syndrome.

Researchers have developed a novel method to estimate the postmortem interval (PM) using microbial communities in a mouse model system, which can accurately estimate PM within 3 days over 48 days. This approach avoids limitations of existing techniques such as forensic entomology, which can have errors ranging from days to months. The study rigorously tested hypotheses about the role of microbes in decomposition and whether changes in communities are predictable over time, crucial for using microbes as a "clock" to estimate PM. 

The study characterized microbial changes in three external and internal sites of 40 destructively sampled mouse corpses over 48 days, distinguishing from prior work which models were evaluated by ability to explain all data points. The results provide a proof-of-principle that microbial ecology of decomposing corpses has potential to be developed into a forensic tool for estimation of PM. A laboratory experiment was conducted to characterize changes in microbial associated with mouse as they decomposed on soil under controlled conditions 48. Bacteria, archaea, and eukaryotes were characterized using combination-independent high-throughput sequencing for each sample.

The relationship between decomposition and postmort microbial communities must be understood to develop a method to PM that is admissible in the justice system. The work indicates decomposition is sufficiently reproducible as an indicator of PM to motivate additional studies. Microbes are ubiquitous in environment, located on humans before death, and can be quantified using high-throughput sequencing. They play an important role in decomposition. Future studies are crucial because environmental parameters such as temperature, moisture, soil, and texture affect rate of decomposition and ability of decomposer to function.

Bacterial associated with decomposing became differentiated from communities over time in abdominal, soil, and skin sites. Consistent shifts in presence and abundance specific taxa occurred during stages decomposition, suggesting succession may be used to estimate PM. A Random Forest model was used to regress PM directly on taxon abundances, a machine technique that creates decision based subsets of features. This approach allowed to assess intra-individual variation of microbiota present in living and mammals extends to community during decomposition.

The study addresses hypotheses that abdominal microbes play a key role in decomposition, contrasting typical studies where only externally accessible sites sampled. When an dies, becomes a resource that supports complex and diverse organisms. This community recycles nutrients, progressing through forensically recognized stages, including Fresh, Active, Decay, Rupt, and Advanced.

Given the role of IN in hypoxic brain damage, the SUM pathway and Na1.2 are identified as potential targets for neuroprotective interventions.

Researchers have developed a mechanistic model to predict the thermal response of Ross River virus (RRV), a mosquito-borne pathogen in Australia and Pacific Islands, and potentially emerging worldwide. The model shows that transmission peaks at moderate temperatures (26.4°C) and declines to zero at thermal limits (17.0 and 31.5°C), accurately predicting year-round endemic in the tropics but seasonal in temperate areas, resulting in nationwide peak human cases. Climate warming will increase transmission in temperate areas (most Australians live) but decrease in tropical areas where temperatures are near thermal optimum. The study highlights the importance of nonlinear models for inferring temperature's role in disease dynamics and predicting climate change.

The model was parameterized with data from two vector species (Cx. annirostris and Ae. vigil) and supports a general pattern of intermediate thermal optima for transmission, where optimal temperature is driven by adult lifespan, but upper and lower limits are uncertain and determined by unique vector-pathogen traits. The strongest signal of temperature on disease is in temperate regions where temperature varies along the rising portion of the R curve (20-25°C). Upper limits of mosquito-borne transmission, a concern for climate change, are determined by vector life traits with symmetrical performance (fecundity and survival) rather than asymmetrical traits (biting or pathogen rate).

The thermal response of RRV matched other mosquitoorne pathogens, but with optimal temperature (26-27°C) within the range found for other diseases (malaria by Anophe spp. at 25, deng and viruses by Ae. aegi and Ae. alb at 29, 26). Statistical analyses of R cases may benefit from nonlinear temperature-dependent R as predictor instead of raw temperature. Breeding habitat drives mosquito abundance and disease, with rainfall linked to R vector abundance and cases. Temperature explains geography of endemic versus seasonal disease and predicts seasonality at national scale.

The model provides a mechanistic link between geographic and seasonal variation in temperature and broad patterns of disease. Nonlinear models are critical for estimating temperature impacts, as effect of few degrees can have positive, negligible, or negative on R along thermal curve. The model assumes temperature drives mosquito density with life trait responses, comparing to alternative where density does not depend on temperature. Most Australians live in temperate areas, transmission is strongly seasonal, with peak two months after population-weighted R, matching prior hypothesized time between temperature and cases.
Researchers investigated the relationship between beta oscillations in the motor cortex and movement by recording neural activity from macaques performing a novel neurofeedback task. The task involved bringing beta power to an instructed state and then executing an arm reach. Results showed that reaches preceded by a reduction in beta power had faster onset times than those preceded by an increase in power. Additionally, neural activity shifted farther from movement onset during beta oscillations, whether induced by neurofeedback or naturally occurring during tasks. This establishes a population basis for slowed movement following beta oscillatory activity.

The study ties together behavioral correlates of beta oscillations with hypotheses of motor cortex encoding movement through population-level activity. Increasing motor beta power with neurofeedback precedes slower onset times, supporting the hypothesis that oscillations are linked to patterns that slow new movements (Gilbertson et al, 2005). Results add to findings of elevated power prior and after-trained movements (Donoghue et al,199; Mur and Fetz,199; Sanes and Donue,199). The behavioral effect of neuro-induced oscillations matches hypotheses of movement-slowing beta during natural movements (Gilbert et al,200), but individual responses during beta activity differ across tasks.

The motor population activity shifts from a threshold when subjects produce oscillations in the task or naturally produce them. Mechanisms of beta used to reconcile discrepancies in reported correlates. Units exhibit different activity during neuro-induced oscillations compared to natural oscillations, but population analyses show consistent patterns. Beta promotes a movement-slow state in both tasks, matching behavior. To explore beta reflect spiking and movement parameters, macaques performed a center-out arm task and beta neuro arm task. Prior to the task, beta limits were computed from the center task.

Neuro-reaching was used with simultaneous multi-scale recordings to study beta's influence on arm behavior and underlying patterns. Results show beta oscillations are a marker of slowed onset. Many tasks engage motor preparation, attention, expectation, and muscular stiffening, suggesting beta reflects states preparing movements. The study proposes shifts in motor neurons reduce accidental movement for encoding specific plans. Neurofeedback investigates population correlates of oscillations, allowing subjects modulate power at rest, choosing strategies for generating beta. This is plausible for oscillations during untrained movements (Donue et al,199; Mur andetz,199b).
TRAF2 is a component of the TNF receptor superfamily and plays a crucial role in regulating immune cell homeostasis. TR2 deficient mice die at birth, so its role in adult tissues is not well-explored. The role of the TR2 RING is controversial, with claims that it cannot function as an ubiquitin E3 ligase but counterclaims it requires sphingosine-1-phosphate, generated by sphingosine kinase 1, to function as E3 ligase. TR2 has tissue-specific functions that do not overlap with sph1.

Synaptic vesicles (SVs) are organelles that store and secrete neurotransmitters at synapses. Following exocyt, their membranes are rapidly recaptured by endocyt and recycled for generation of new fusion-competent SVs. Clath-mediated endocyt (CME) plays a key role in endytic traffic. Dynamin 1 and dynamin 3, encoded by DNM1 and D3 genes, respectively, are involved in endytic membrane fission. However, their role in bulkocyt and reformation of SV from endosomes is unclear. Bulkocyt occurs in response to strong stimulus and is dynamin 3 dependent, but dyn 1 is not required. Dynamin 1/3 double KO synapses show massive bulkoc formation, but impaired conversion of endosomes to SV. Clath heavy chain (CH) KD in Dyn/3 KO neurons does not further impair conversion of endosomes to SV, indicating clath independence. Bulkoc and conversion of endosomes to SV occur in Dyn/ KO synapses, less efficiently than in WT syn. Clath KD in Dyn/ KO does not further impair conversion, suggesting clath independence. Bulkoc involves membrane remodeling and fission, with dynamin GTPase mediating endytic f in multiple contexts, including endytic f at synapses. However, results are conflicting. Endytic intermediates generated by bulkoc, bulkosomes, disappear as new SV appear, suggesting precursor-product relationship and direct formation of SV from bulkosomes. To address clath independence of SV formation from bulkosomes, CH KD was performed using lentivirus strategy. CH KD in Dyn/ KO does not impair conversion of end to SV, indicating clath independence. Bulkoc recapture of SV membranes by stimulus is not impaired in syn lacking dyn 1 and 3, where C is severely perturbed.

They used live imaging of the mTOR component RAP and a cell-permeant fluorescent analogue diucine ester to examine the spatial and temporal dynamics of mTOR1 translocation to lysosomes.  The study used a series of leuc analogues for m1 activation and found that lysosomes crucial for activation but the dynamics of process may be complicated.
